# A Comprehensive Survey on Upper Limb Exoskeleton Design

## 1 Introduction

### 1.1 Background and Motivation

The growing importance of upper limb exoskeletons in addressing mobility challenges is a testament to their transformative potential in various domains, particularly in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive contexts. As the global population ages and the prevalence of conditions such as stroke, spinal cord injuries, and neurological disorders continues to rise, the demand for effective mobility assistance solutions has never been higher. Upper limb exoskeletons have emerged as a promising technology to tackle these challenges by offering targeted support and rehabilitation to individuals with motor impairments. Their ability to provide precise, repeatable, and personalized assistance makes them a vital component in the evolving landscape of assistive and rehabilitative technologies [1].

In the rehabilitation context, upper limb exoskeletons are playing a critical role in aiding patients recovering from neurological conditions. Traditional rehabilitation methods often rely on manual therapy, which can be labor-intensive and may not provide the consistent and intensive training necessary for optimal recovery. Exoskeletons offer a more systematic approach by enabling repetitive, controlled, and task-specific training. For instance, the integration of intelligent control systems, such as those based on machine learning and deep learning, allows exoskeletons to adapt to individual patient needs, thereby improving the effectiveness of rehabilitation programs [2]. These systems can analyze sensor data in real-time to adjust assistance levels and ensure that patients receive the appropriate level of support during their recovery process.

In industrial settings, upper limb exoskeletons are being increasingly adopted to enhance worker productivity and reduce physical strain. Tasks that involve repetitive motions, heavy lifting, or prolonged exposure to awkward postures can lead to musculoskeletal injuries and fatigue. Exoskeletons designed for industrial use provide targeted support to the arms and shoulders, reducing the workload on the user's muscles and joints. For example, voice-controlled upper body exoskeletons have been developed to assist workers in lifting tasks, offering up to 20 kg of additional strength and significantly reducing the physical strain associated with repetitive movements [3]. Such technologies not only improve worker safety but also increase efficiency, making them a valuable asset in modern manufacturing and logistics environments.

The assistive context is another area where upper limb exoskeletons are making a significant impact. For individuals with motor disabilities, these devices can provide the necessary support to perform daily tasks, thereby enhancing their independence and quality of life. Soft exosuits, for instance, are being developed to offer flexible, wearable support that can be easily adapted to different user needs. These exosuits are designed to be lightweight, portable, and comfortable, making them suitable for long-term use in both clinical and home settings [1]. The integration of soft robotics and AI technologies enables these exosuits to provide intuitive and responsive assistance, allowing users to perform tasks with greater ease and confidence.

The development of upper limb exoskeletons is driven by a confluence of technological advancements and a growing understanding of human biomechanics. Innovations in materials science, actuation technologies, and control systems have enabled the creation of more sophisticated and user-friendly exoskeletons. For example, the use of shape-memory alloys and pneumatic actuators has allowed for the development of lightweight and compliant systems that can adapt to the user's movements [4]. Additionally, the integration of real-time sensing and feedback systems, such as electromyography (EMG) and force sensors, has improved the accuracy and responsiveness of exoskeletons, enabling them to provide more effective assistance [5].

Moreover, the interdisciplinary nature of upper limb exoskeleton design has fostered collaboration between researchers in robotics, biomechanics, neuroscience, and artificial intelligence. This multidisciplinary approach has led to the development of more comprehensive and effective solutions that address the complex needs of users. For instance, the use of neuromechanical models and adaptive control strategies has enabled exoskeletons to better understand and respond to human movement patterns, thereby enhancing their performance and usability [6].

Despite these advancements, there are still significant challenges that need to be addressed to fully realize the potential of upper limb exoskeletons. Issues such as ergonomics, adaptability, and scalability remain critical concerns. For example, the design of exoskeletons that can accommodate a wide range of human anatomies and movement patterns is a complex task that requires careful consideration of biomechanical principles and user feedback [7]. Additionally, the integration of AI and machine learning into exoskeleton systems presents both opportunities and challenges, as these technologies must be carefully calibrated to ensure they enhance, rather than hinder, user experience [2].

In conclusion, the growing importance of upper limb exoskeletons in addressing mobility challenges is a clear indication of their potential to revolutionize rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive care. As research continues to advance, it is essential to focus on overcoming existing challenges and exploring new opportunities to make these technologies more accessible, effective, and user-friendly. The future of upper limb exoskeletons holds great promise, and with continued innovation and collaboration, they have the potential to significantly improve the lives of countless individuals.

### 1.2 Applications and Current Challenges

Upper limb exoskeletons have emerged as transformative technologies with a wide range of applications, ranging from rehabilitation of stroke patients to industrial assistance and support in daily living activities. These devices are designed to assist or augment human upper limb functionality, enabling users to perform tasks more efficiently and with reduced physical strain. In rehabilitation, exoskeletons play a critical role in aiding patients who have suffered from neurological conditions such as stroke, spinal cord injuries, or other motor impairments. They provide repetitive, targeted, and structured training to help restore motor function and improve muscle strength [8]. Additionally, in industrial settings, upper limb exoskeletons assist workers in performing physically demanding tasks, such as lifting heavy objects or maintaining awkward postures for extended periods, thereby reducing the risk of musculoskeletal injuries and enhancing productivity [3]. Furthermore, in daily life, these exoskeletons support individuals with motor disabilities in performing routine activities, such as grasping objects, eating, or dressing, thereby improving their independence and quality of life [9].

Despite these promising applications, the design and implementation of upper limb exoskeletons face significant challenges that need to be addressed to ensure their effectiveness, safety, and widespread adoption. One of the primary challenges is achieving a balance between the exoskeleton’s mechanical performance and user comfort. Current designs often struggle to provide sufficient assistance while remaining lightweight and non-intrusive, as bulky or heavy exoskeletons can hinder mobility and cause discomfort during prolonged use [7]. Additionally, ensuring seamless human-robot interaction remains a major obstacle. Exoskeletons must be able to interpret user intent accurately and respond in real-time to provide timely assistance, which is challenging due to the complexity of human movement and variability in user capabilities [10].

Another critical challenge is the integration of advanced sensing and control systems. While technologies such as electromyography (EMG), force sensors, and vision-based systems have been explored to enhance the exoskeleton’s ability to detect user intent and adapt to changing conditions, these systems often face issues with signal noise, sensor placement, and user variability. For instance, EMG-based control systems may not always provide reliable intent recognition due to the variability in muscle signals across different users [11]. Moreover, the real-time processing of sensor data is computationally demanding, and delays in response can impact the effectiveness of the exoskeleton in dynamic environments [5].

Energy efficiency and power management also pose significant challenges, particularly for portable and wearable exoskeletons. The need for sustained operation without frequent recharging or cumbersome power sources limits the practicality of these devices in real-world scenarios. Additionally, the integration of soft and compliant actuation mechanisms, such as pneumatic or dielectric elastomer actuators, requires careful optimization to ensure both performance and safety [4]. These actuators offer advantages in terms of weight and adaptability but may lack the precision and reliability of traditional rigid actuators, making them less suitable for high-stakes applications.

Safety and robustness are also major concerns, especially in clinical and industrial settings where the consequences of system failure can be severe. Ensuring that exoskeletons operate reliably under diverse conditions and user interactions remains a complex task. For example, the risk of collision with objects or unintended movements can lead to injury, necessitating advanced control strategies and fail-safe mechanisms [12]. Furthermore, the variability in human anatomy and movement patterns makes it difficult to design exoskeletons that are universally adaptable, leading to the need for personalized and customizable solutions [13].

In addition to technical challenges, the adoption of upper limb exoskeletons is also hindered by economic and practical barriers. The cost of development, manufacturing, and maintenance can be prohibitively high, limiting access for many users. Moreover, the lack of standardized evaluation and certification protocols makes it difficult to assess the efficacy and reliability of different exoskeleton designs [14]. These challenges highlight the need for further research and collaboration across disciplines to develop exoskeletons that are not only technically advanced but also practical, affordable, and user-friendly.

In summary, upper limb exoskeletons hold great promise in enhancing rehabilitation, industrial productivity, and daily living support. However, their successful implementation requires overcoming significant challenges in design, control, user interaction, and safety. By addressing these issues through interdisciplinary research and innovation, the potential of upper limb exoskeletons can be fully realized, leading to broader adoption and greater impact on human health and well-being.

### 1.3 Evolution of Upper Limb Exoskeleton Technologies

The evolution of upper limb exoskeleton technologies has been marked by significant advancements in materials, actuation, and control mechanisms, driven by the need to address mobility challenges in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive contexts. Initially, upper limb exoskeletons were primarily rigid, mechanical systems designed to provide passive support or assistive force. These early systems were characterized by their use of heavy metals and rigid structures, which limited their portability and usability. As research progressed, the development of lighter and more flexible materials, such as composites and polymers, allowed for the creation of more wearable and less intrusive exoskeletons [15].

One of the key innovations in the evolution of upper limb exoskeletons has been the integration of advanced actuation technologies. Early exoskeletons relied on traditional electric motors and hydraulic systems, which were often bulky and energy-intensive. However, the emergence of more compact and efficient actuation systems, such as series elastic actuators (SEAs), pneumatic actuators, and shape-memory alloys, has significantly improved the performance and usability of these devices. For example, SEAs have been widely adopted for their ability to provide compliance and energy efficiency, making them suitable for applications where human-robot interaction is critical [16]. Pneumatic actuators, on the other hand, offer lightweight and compliant actuation, which is particularly beneficial in soft exoskeletons designed for rehabilitation and assistance [1].

In addition to advancements in actuation, the development of more sophisticated control mechanisms has played a crucial role in the evolution of upper limb exoskeletons. Traditional control systems were often based on fixed, pre-programmed trajectories, which limited the adaptability of these devices. However, the introduction of adaptive control strategies, such as impedance control and model-based control, has enabled exoskeletons to dynamically adjust to user needs and environmental changes. Impedance control, for instance, allows exoskeletons to regulate the interaction forces between the user and the device, enhancing safety and comfort [15]. Model-based control strategies, which utilize mathematical models of the exoskeleton and the user, have also been instrumental in improving the accuracy and reliability of exoskeleton performance [17].

The integration of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) has further transformed the field of upper limb exoskeletons. These technologies have enabled the development of intelligent control systems that can learn from user behavior and adapt to individual needs. For example, reinforcement learning has been used to optimize the control of exoskeletons for tasks such as walking and lifting, allowing for more natural and efficient movement [18]. Similarly, deep learning techniques have been employed to improve the accuracy of intent recognition, enabling exoskeletons to respond to user commands with greater precision [2].

Another significant advancement in the evolution of upper limb exoskeletons has been the development of soft robotic technologies. Traditional rigid exoskeletons were often uncomfortable and restrictive, limiting their use in real-world applications. Soft exoskeletons, on the other hand, are designed to be more flexible and adaptable, providing a more natural and comfortable experience for users. These systems often utilize materials such as fabrics and elastomers, which can conform to the user's body and reduce the risk of injury [1]. The use of soft robotics has also enabled the creation of more wearable and less obtrusive exoskeletons, which are particularly beneficial for long-term use in rehabilitation and daily activities.

The evolution of upper limb exoskeleton technologies has also been influenced by the need for better sensing and feedback systems. Early exoskeletons lacked the ability to provide real-time feedback, which limited their effectiveness in dynamic environments. The integration of advanced sensors, such as electromyography (EMG) and force sensors, has enabled the development of more responsive and adaptive exoskeletons. These sensors allow exoskeletons to detect user intent and adjust their assistance accordingly, improving the overall user experience [2]. Additionally, the use of vision-based systems and multimodal sensor fusion has enhanced the ability of exoskeletons to interact with their environment and provide more accurate and reliable feedback.

The ongoing evolution of upper limb exoskeleton technologies has also been driven by the need for more efficient and sustainable designs. The development of energy-efficient actuators and control systems has been a key focus, as it reduces the power consumption and extends the operational life of these devices. For example, the use of variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) has enabled the creation of exoskeletons that can modulate their stiffness to optimize performance and reduce energy consumption [19]. Additionally, the integration of energy recovery systems, such as regenerative braking, has further improved the efficiency of exoskeletons, making them more viable for long-term use.

In conclusion, the evolution of upper limb exoskeleton technologies has been a dynamic process characterized by continuous innovation in materials, actuation, and control mechanisms. These advancements have not only improved the performance and usability of exoskeletons but also expanded their applications in rehabilitation, industry, and daily life. The integration of AI and machine learning has further enhanced the adaptability and intelligence of these devices, paving the way for more personalized and effective solutions. As research continues, the development of more efficient, comfortable, and intelligent exoskeletons will play a crucial role in addressing the growing demand for mobility assistance and rehabilitation technologies.

### 1.4 Multidisciplinary Nature of the Field

The design of upper limb exoskeletons is inherently a multidisciplinary endeavor, drawing upon a rich tapestry of knowledge from various scientific and engineering domains. This complexity arises from the need to create devices that not only support and augment human limb function but also seamlessly integrate with the user's physiology, cognition, and environment. As a result, the development of upper limb exoskeletons requires the synthesis of expertise from robotics, biomechanics, neuroscience, artificial intelligence, and other related disciplines. Each of these fields contributes unique insights and methodologies that are essential for the design, functionality, and effectiveness of these devices. 

One of the key areas of collaboration is robotics, which provides the foundational principles for the mechanical design and control of exoskeletons. Robotic systems are designed to interact with humans in a safe and efficient manner, which is crucial for the success of upper limb exoskeletons. The field of robotics contributes methodologies for motion planning, control algorithms, and actuation systems that enable exoskeletons to perform complex tasks. For instance, the integration of series elastic actuators (SEAs) and pneumatic artificial muscles (PAMs) in exoskeletons has been extensively explored in the literature, as these actuators offer a balance between compliance and strength, which is essential for human-robot interaction [20]. 

Biomechanics plays a critical role in understanding the mechanics of human movement and the physiological constraints of the upper limb. This field provides the necessary knowledge to design exoskeletons that are ergonomically sound and capable of mimicking natural human motion. Biomechanical principles guide the development of kinematic models that describe the movement of the upper limb, ensuring that the exoskeleton does not interfere with the user's natural range of motion. For example, the systematic approach to kinematic design for upper limb rehabilitation exoskeletons has been proposed to address the challenge of creating devices that can effectively support a wide range of movement patterns [21]. 

Neuroscience is another vital discipline that contributes to the design of upper limb exoskeletons. Understanding the neural mechanisms underlying motor control and sensory feedback is essential for developing exoskeletons that can effectively interpret and respond to user intent. The integration of electromyography (EMG) sensors and other neural interfaces allows exoskeletons to detect muscle activity and translate it into control signals, enabling more intuitive and responsive interaction. For instance, the use of cloud-based deep learning to predict human intention for strength augmentation has been demonstrated in an intelligent upper-limb exoskeleton system [2]. This approach highlights the importance of neuroscience in enabling exoskeletons to adapt to the user's needs in real-time.

Artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning are increasingly being leveraged to enhance the functionality and adaptability of upper limb exoskeletons. These technologies enable exoskeletons to learn from user data and improve their performance over time. AI-driven control strategies, such as model predictive control (MPC) and reinforcement learning, have been explored to optimize the interaction between the exoskeleton and the user. For example, the application of model predictive control (MPC) in lower limb exoskeletons has been demonstrated to provide human-centered assistance by adapting to different modes of interaction [17]. Additionally, the use of machine learning for real-time data processing and signal interpretation has been highlighted as a critical component in the development of intelligent exoskeletons [5].

The interdisciplinary nature of upper limb exoskeleton design is also evident in the integration of soft robotics and wearable technologies. Soft exoskeletons, which are made from flexible materials, offer a more comfortable and less restrictive alternative to traditional rigid exoskeletons. These devices can be tailored to the user's anatomy and provide a more natural range of motion. The development of soft robotic exoskeletons, such as the fabric-based pneumatic actuators, has been a significant area of research, with the aim of creating devices that are both functional and user-friendly [22]. Furthermore, the use of soft wearable sensors to provide sensory feedback has been shown to enhance the performance of exoskeletons by enabling more accurate and responsive control [5].

In addition to these technical disciplines, the design of upper limb exoskeletons also requires an understanding of human factors and user experience. This involves considering the psychological and social aspects of using an exoskeleton, as well as the practical challenges of integrating such devices into daily life. The development of exoskeletons that are not only technically advanced but also user-friendly and socially acceptable is a critical challenge. Research in this area has highlighted the importance of user-centered design, emphasizing the need to involve end-users in the design process to ensure that the final product meets their needs and preferences [23].

The multidisciplinary nature of upper limb exoskeleton design is further underscored by the need for collaboration across different research communities. The integration of insights from robotics, biomechanics, neuroscience, and AI requires a coordinated effort to address the complex challenges involved in creating effective and user-friendly exoskeletons. This collaboration is essential for advancing the field and ensuring that exoskeletons can be widely adopted in various applications, from rehabilitation to industrial settings.

In conclusion, the design of upper limb exoskeletons is a multifaceted endeavor that requires the integration of knowledge from multiple disciplines. The synergy between robotics, biomechanics, neuroscience, and artificial intelligence is crucial for developing exoskeletons that are not only technically advanced but also capable of enhancing human performance in a safe and intuitive manner. As the field continues to evolve, the importance of interdisciplinary collaboration will only grow, driving innovation and improving the lives of those who rely on these technologies.

### 1.5 Objectives of the Survey

The objective of this survey is to provide a comprehensive and systematic review of the current state of upper limb exoskeleton design, examining research trends, identifying technological gaps, and offering a foundation for future research and development in the field. Upper limb exoskeletons have garnered significant attention due to their potential to improve the quality of life for individuals with motor impairments, enhance human performance in industrial applications, and support rehabilitation processes. However, despite the growing interest and technological advancements, there is a need for a structured and detailed analysis of the field to identify key challenges and opportunities for innovation. This survey aims to address these needs by synthesizing existing research, highlighting critical issues, and suggesting directions for future exploration.  

One of the primary objectives of this survey is to examine current research trends in upper limb exoskeleton design. The field has evolved significantly over the past decade, with advances in actuation technologies, control strategies, and sensor integration. The survey will explore the evolution of upper limb exoskeletons, focusing on how different design philosophies—such as rigid, soft, and wearable systems—have emerged to meet specific functional and application requirements. It will also highlight the integration of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning in control systems, which has the potential to enhance user interaction, adaptability, and overall performance [24]. By analyzing recent studies, the survey will provide insights into the most promising research directions and the underlying principles that drive innovation in this domain.  

Another key objective is to identify technological gaps that hinder the widespread adoption and effectiveness of upper limb exoskeletons. Despite significant progress, several challenges remain, including issues related to ergonomics, weight distribution, user adaptability, and energy efficiency. For example, current exoskeletons often struggle with maintaining user comfort over prolonged use, which limits their practicality in real-world applications [25]. Additionally, the integration of advanced control systems, such as impedance control and model-based approaches, presents challenges in terms of computational complexity and real-time adaptability [26]. The survey will critically evaluate these challenges and assess how they impact the performance, usability, and reliability of exoskeleton systems. By doing so, it will provide a clear understanding of the technological hurdles that need to be addressed for the next generation of exoskeletons.  

In addition to examining current trends and identifying gaps, the survey aims to provide a foundation for future research and development in the field. This includes proposing new research directions, suggesting improvements to existing designs, and encouraging interdisciplinary collaboration. For instance, the integration of multimodal sensing and feedback systems has the potential to enhance the accuracy and responsiveness of exoskeletons, particularly in dynamic and complex environments [5]. The survey will explore the role of sensor fusion, real-time data processing, and adaptive control in improving the user experience and expanding the applications of upper limb exoskeletons. Furthermore, it will highlight the importance of considering user intent recognition and personalization, as these factors are critical for ensuring the effectiveness and usability of exoskeletons in rehabilitation and assistive settings [27].  

Another important objective is to assess the impact of emerging technologies on the development of upper limb exoskeletons. For example, the use of soft robotics and flexible actuators has opened new possibilities for creating lightweight, adaptable, and user-friendly systems [4]. The survey will investigate how these technologies can be leveraged to overcome traditional limitations, such as rigidity and inflexibility, and to improve the overall user experience. Additionally, it will examine the role of artificial intelligence in enabling more intuitive and responsive control, particularly in applications that require real-time adaptation to changing user conditions [28]. By highlighting these advancements, the survey aims to demonstrate how cutting-edge technologies can be harnessed to enhance the performance and functionality of upper limb exoskeletons.  

Moreover, the survey seeks to contribute to the broader discourse on the ethical, social, and environmental implications of upper limb exoskeletons. As these systems become more sophisticated and widely used, it is essential to consider their impact on users, healthcare systems, and society as a whole [29]. The survey will explore the ethical considerations surrounding the use of AI and machine learning in exoskeleton control, as well as the potential for these technologies to reduce disparities in access to assistive devices. It will also address the environmental impact of exoskeleton production and usage, emphasizing the need for sustainable design practices that minimize resource consumption and waste [30]. By taking a holistic approach, the survey will provide a comprehensive view of the field and its broader implications.  

Finally, the survey aims to serve as a resource for researchers, engineers, and practitioners working in the field of upper limb exoskeletons. It will provide a detailed overview of the current state of the art, identify key research challenges, and suggest directions for future innovation. By synthesizing the findings of existing studies and highlighting emerging trends, the survey will help to guide the development of more effective, user-centric, and sustainable exoskeleton systems. Ultimately, the objective of this survey is to contribute to the advancement of upper limb exoskeleton technology by fostering collaboration, encouraging interdisciplinary research, and promoting the development of solutions that meet the needs of diverse user populations.

## 2 Overview of Upper Limb Exoskeleton Technologies

### 2.1 Classification of Upper Limb Exoskeletons

Upper limb exoskeletons are wearable robotic systems designed to enhance human performance, provide assistance, or aid in rehabilitation. These systems are classified based on their degree of freedom (DoF), application context, and design type. Understanding these classifications is essential for evaluating the suitability of exoskeletons for specific tasks and user requirements. The classification of upper limb exoskeletons is influenced by the diverse applications they serve, including rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive contexts. Additionally, their design types—rigid, soft, or wearable—play a critical role in determining their functionality, comfort, and adaptability. This subsection explores these classifications in detail, highlighting their roles and the research that supports them.

Upper limb exoskeletons are often classified by their degree of freedom, which refers to the number of independent movements they can perform. This classification is crucial for determining the range of motion and the complexity of the tasks the exoskeleton can assist with. For example, exoskeletons with a higher number of degrees of freedom are better suited for tasks requiring intricate movements, such as grasping or manipulating objects. Research in this area has shown that exoskeletons with a higher degree of freedom can offer more precise control over the user's limb, which is particularly beneficial in rehabilitation applications [18]. However, a higher degree of freedom can also lead to increased complexity in control systems, making the design and implementation more challenging. In contrast, exoskeletons with fewer degrees of freedom are often simpler and more cost-effective, making them suitable for industrial or assistive applications where the required movements are more limited [31].

In addition to the degree of freedom, upper limb exoskeletons are classified based on their application context. These contexts include rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications. Rehabilitation exoskeletons are designed to aid in the recovery of motor functions for patients with neurological conditions such as stroke or spinal cord injuries. These exoskeletons are often equipped with advanced sensors and control systems to provide personalized assistance and track the user's progress [18]. On the other hand, industrial exoskeletons are designed to reduce physical strain on workers by providing support during repetitive or heavy tasks. These exoskeletons are typically more robust and durable, as they are intended for use in demanding environments [3]. Assistive exoskeletons, on the other hand, are designed to aid individuals with motor impairments in performing daily tasks. These exoskeletons often emphasize comfort and ease of use, as they are intended for long-term wear [31].

The design type of an upper limb exoskeleton is another critical classification criterion. Exoskeletons can be categorized as rigid, soft, or wearable, each offering distinct advantages and disadvantages. Rigid exoskeletons are typically constructed using metal or composite materials and are designed to provide strong mechanical support. These exoskeletons are well-suited for industrial applications where durability and strength are paramount. However, rigid exoskeletons can be heavy and less comfortable, making them less suitable for long-term use in rehabilitation or assistive contexts [31]. Soft exoskeletons, on the other hand, are made from flexible materials and are designed to provide assistance while minimizing discomfort. These exoskeletons are particularly beneficial in rehabilitation, as they can conform to the user's body and provide a more natural range of motion [8]. Wearable exoskeletons are a hybrid design that combines the benefits of both rigid and soft exoskeletons. These exoskeletons are designed to be lightweight and comfortable while still providing sufficient support for the user's movements [31].

The classification of upper limb exoskeletons based on their degree of freedom, application context, and design type is essential for understanding their capabilities and limitations. Each classification criterion influences the choice of exoskeleton for specific tasks, and the research in this area provides valuable insights into the design and functionality of these systems. For example, the development of soft exoskeletons has been driven by the need to provide more comfortable and adaptable solutions for rehabilitation and assistive applications [8]. Similarly, the integration of advanced control systems in rehabilitation exoskeletons has been a key focus of research, as it enables more personalized and effective assistance for patients [18].

In conclusion, the classification of upper limb exoskeletons based on their degree of freedom, application context, and design type is essential for evaluating their suitability for various tasks and user requirements. The research in this area has provided valuable insights into the design and functionality of these systems, highlighting the importance of considering these classifications when developing and deploying exoskeletons for specific applications. As the field continues to evolve, the classification of exoskeletons will remain a critical aspect of their design and implementation, ensuring that they meet the diverse needs of users in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive contexts.

### 2.2 Key Components of Upper Limb Exoskeletons

[32]

The design and functionality of upper limb exoskeletons rely on several essential components, each playing a critical role in ensuring effective and safe interaction between the device and the user. These components include actuators, sensors, structural frameworks, and control systems, all of which contribute to the overall performance and adaptability of the exoskeleton. Understanding these key components is crucial for the development of advanced exoskeleton systems that can meet the diverse needs of users in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive contexts.

Actuators are the core components that provide the mechanical power necessary for movement and assistance. They convert energy into motion, enabling the exoskeleton to perform tasks such as lifting, grasping, and supporting the user's limb. Various types of actuators have been employed in upper limb exoskeletons, including series elastic actuators (SEAs), pneumatic actuators, and shape-memory alloys (SMAs). SEAs, for instance, are known for their ability to reduce motor energy consumption and peak power by incorporating an elastic element that stores and releases energy [4]. Pneumatic actuators, on the other hand, offer lightweight and compliant solutions, making them suitable for applications where user comfort and adaptability are essential [8]. SMAs, which can generate high forces and displacements, are also being explored for their potential in creating more compact and efficient exoskeleton designs [4]. The selection of actuator type depends on the specific requirements of the exoskeleton, including the desired level of force, speed, and precision.

Sensors are another critical component, as they enable the exoskeleton to monitor and respond to user movements and environmental conditions. Sensors provide real-time feedback, which is essential for accurate control and safe operation. Common types of sensors used in upper limb exoskeletons include electromyography (EMG) sensors, force sensors, and vision-based systems. EMG sensors capture muscle activity, allowing the exoskeleton to recognize user intent and adjust its assistance accordingly [11]. Force sensors, such as strain gauges and tactile sensors, measure the forces exerted by the user and the environment, ensuring that the exoskeleton can provide appropriate support while avoiding excessive resistance [33]. Vision-based systems, including cameras and depth sensors, are also being integrated to enhance the exoskeleton's awareness of its surroundings, enabling tasks such as object recognition and gesture-based interaction [10]. The combination of these sensors allows the exoskeleton to provide a more intuitive and responsive experience for the user.

The structural framework of an upper limb exoskeleton is designed to provide mechanical support and ensure proper alignment with the user's anatomy. This framework typically includes a series of joints, links, and connectors that mimic the movement of the human limb. The structural design must balance strength, flexibility, and comfort, as it directly affects the user's ability to perform tasks and the overall usability of the exoskeleton. Materials such as lightweight alloys, carbon fiber composites, and soft textiles are often used to achieve this balance [34]. Additionally, the structural framework must be adjustable to accommodate different user sizes and anatomical variations, ensuring that the exoskeleton can be customized for individual needs [34]. The design of the structural framework also plays a role in determining the exoskeleton's compliance and backdrivability, which are important for user safety and ease of use [34].

Control systems are the brain of the upper limb exoskeleton, responsible for processing sensor data, making decisions, and coordinating the actions of the actuators. These systems must be capable of handling complex tasks, such as adaptive control, impedance control, and real-time adjustments based on user feedback. Control strategies can range from traditional PID control to more advanced techniques, such as model predictive control and machine learning-based approaches [35]. The choice of control system depends on the specific application and the level of autonomy required. For example, impedance control is often used to manage interaction forces between the exoskeleton and the user, ensuring a safe and adaptable human-robot interaction [35]. Machine learning techniques, on the other hand, enable the exoskeleton to learn from user data and improve its performance over time [36]. The integration of control systems with the other components of the exoskeleton is essential for achieving seamless and effective operation.

The integration of these key components—actuators, sensors, structural frameworks, and control systems—demonstrates the complexity and multifaceted nature of upper limb exoskeleton design. Each component must be carefully selected and optimized to ensure that the exoskeleton can perform its intended functions effectively and safely. For instance, the actuator must provide sufficient force and precision, the sensors must accurately monitor user and environmental conditions, the structural framework must ensure proper alignment and comfort, and the control system must enable adaptive and responsive behavior [4]. The interplay between these components is crucial for the overall performance of the exoskeleton, as any deficiency in one area can negatively impact the system's functionality.

Furthermore, the design of these components must also consider factors such as energy efficiency, robustness, and user adaptability. Energy efficiency is a critical concern, as it directly affects the autonomy and usability of the exoskeleton [4]. Robustness is essential to ensure that the exoskeleton can operate reliably in various environments and under different conditions [4]. User adaptability is also important, as the exoskeleton must be able to adjust to individual user needs and preferences [37]. The ongoing advancements in materials science, sensor technology, and control algorithms are continuously improving the performance of these components, leading to more effective and user-friendly exoskeleton systems.

In conclusion, the key components of upper limb exoskeletons—actuators, sensors, structural frameworks, and control systems—are integral to the design and functionality of these devices. Each component plays a unique role in ensuring that the exoskeleton can provide the necessary support and assistance to the user. The development and optimization of these components are essential for the continued advancement of upper limb exoskeleton technology, enabling more effective solutions for rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications. As research in this field progresses, the integration of these components will become even more sophisticated, leading to more advanced and user-centric exoskeleton systems.

### 2.3 Rehabilitation Applications

Upper limb exoskeletons have gained significant attention in rehabilitation settings due to their potential to aid in the recovery of motor functions for individuals with neurological and musculoskeletal impairments. These devices are designed to provide assistance and support during rehabilitation exercises, enabling patients to perform repetitive and targeted movements that are crucial for motor recovery. Rehabilitation applications of upper limb exoskeletons include aiding stroke recovery, spinal cord injuries, and other motor impairments, with a growing body of evidence from clinical trials and case studies supporting their effectiveness.

One of the most prominent applications of upper limb exoskeletons is in stroke rehabilitation. Stroke often leads to motor impairments in the upper limbs, which can significantly impact a patient's ability to perform daily activities. Exoskeletons can provide assistance during repetitive and task-specific training, which has been shown to improve motor function and functional outcomes. For instance, a study on the CLEVERarm, a rehabilitation exoskeleton, demonstrated that it could effectively assist patients in regaining motor function through controlled and guided movements [38]. The CLEVERarm utilizes a combination of mechanical support and sensory feedback to facilitate motor recovery, highlighting the potential of exoskeletons in stroke rehabilitation.

Spinal cord injuries (SCIs) also benefit from the use of upper limb exoskeletons. Patients with SCIs often experience severe motor impairments, making it difficult for them to perform even basic tasks. Upper limb exoskeletons can provide the necessary support to enable these individuals to regain some degree of independence. A notable example is the use of exoskeletons in helping individuals with SCIs perform daily tasks, such as reaching, grasping, and manipulating objects. The development of modular and customizable exoskeletons has further enhanced their applicability in this context, allowing for personalized assistance based on the specific needs of the patient [23].

In addition to stroke and spinal cord injuries, upper limb exoskeletons are also used in the rehabilitation of other motor impairments, such as those resulting from cerebral palsy, Parkinson's disease, and traumatic brain injuries. These devices offer targeted assistance in improving range of motion, strength, and coordination. For instance, the use of soft robotic exoskeletons has shown promise in providing gentle and adaptive support for patients with neuromuscular disorders. A case study on the development of a fabric-based pneumatic actuator for hand rehabilitation demonstrated that such exoskeletons could effectively support hand movements and improve grip strength [39]. The integration of soft robotics in exoskeleton design allows for greater comfort and adaptability, making them suitable for long-term use in rehabilitation settings.

Clinical trials have also demonstrated the effectiveness of upper limb exoskeletons in improving patient outcomes. For example, a study on the use of a portable elbow exoskeleton with a Twisted Strings Actuator (TSA) showed that it could provide significant assistance to patients with upper limb impairments, enabling them to perform daily tasks more effectively [40]. The TSA-based exoskeleton was found to be lightweight and efficient, making it a viable option for home use and outpatient rehabilitation.

Another important aspect of rehabilitation applications is the integration of advanced control systems and machine learning algorithms to enhance the performance of exoskeletons. For instance, the use of deep learning in the intelligent upper-limb exoskeleton described in a recent study demonstrated that such devices could predict human intention and provide adaptive assistance based on real-time sensory feedback [2]. This approach not only improves the effectiveness of rehabilitation but also enhances the user experience by making the exoskeleton more intuitive and responsive.

Furthermore, the use of virtual reality (VR) in conjunction with exoskeletons has shown promising results in rehabilitation settings. A case study on the integration of VR with a robotic device (KinArm) for upper extremity rehabilitation highlighted the potential of VR in enhancing patient engagement and motivation during rehabilitation sessions [41]. The combination of VR and exoskeletons creates an immersive and interactive environment, which can make rehabilitation exercises more engaging and effective.

The design and implementation of upper limb exoskeletons also benefit from the use of multimodal sensor fusion, which combines data from multiple sensors to improve the accuracy and reliability of the exoskeleton's feedback system. This approach has been explored in various studies, including the use of EMG, force, and tactile sensors to provide real-time feedback and enhance the control of the exoskeleton [5]. By integrating multiple sensor modalities, exoskeletons can better understand the user's intent and provide more precise assistance, leading to improved rehabilitation outcomes.

The effectiveness of upper limb exoskeletons in rehabilitation settings is further supported by case studies and clinical trials that have demonstrated their ability to improve functional outcomes. For example, a case study on the use of a haptic exoskeleton in contact-rich tasks showed that it could enhance task performance and reduce the physical strain on users [15]. The exoskeleton was able to provide real-time feedback and support, making it an effective tool for rehabilitation.

In addition, the integration of control strategies such as impedance control and adaptive control has been shown to enhance the performance of upper limb exoskeletons. Impedance control allows the exoskeleton to adjust its stiffness and damping in response to user movements, providing a more natural and intuitive interaction [26]. Adaptive control, on the other hand, enables the exoskeleton to dynamically adjust its parameters based on the user's condition and task requirements, leading to improved performance and user satisfaction.

Overall, the rehabilitation applications of upper limb exoskeletons are diverse and promising. These devices offer a range of benefits, including improved motor function, increased independence, and enhanced user engagement. The integration of advanced technologies such as machine learning, VR, and multimodal sensors has further enhanced their effectiveness in rehabilitation settings. As research continues to advance, the potential for upper limb exoskeletons to revolutionize rehabilitation and improve the quality of life for individuals with motor impairments is becoming increasingly evident.

### 2.4 Industrial and Assistive Applications

Upper limb exoskeletons have found significant applications in both industrial and assistive contexts, addressing challenges related to worker fatigue, injury prevention, and enhancing the quality of life for individuals with motor disabilities. These devices are designed to provide mechanical assistance, thereby reducing the physical burden on users and improving their overall performance in tasks that require repetitive or strenuous upper limb movements.

In industrial settings, upper limb exoskeletons play a crucial role in mitigating the physical strain on workers, particularly in tasks that involve lifting, assembly, and repetitive motions. The integration of exoskeletons into industrial environments has the potential to reduce musculoskeletal injuries and increase productivity. For instance, the development of lightweight and ergonomic exoskeletons has enabled workers to perform tasks with reduced physical effort. One example is the use of exoskeletons in automotive manufacturing, where they assist workers in lifting heavy parts and maintaining proper posture during repetitive assembly tasks. This application not only reduces the risk of injuries but also enhances the efficiency of the workforce. Research indicates that the use of such exoskeletons can significantly reduce muscle activity and perceived exertion, thereby improving worker comfort and job satisfaction [42].

Moreover, the application of upper limb exoskeletons in industrial contexts extends beyond mere physical assistance. These devices are increasingly being integrated with advanced sensor technologies and control systems to enable real-time monitoring and adjustment of the user's movements. For instance, the use of force and tactile sensors allows the exoskeleton to respond dynamically to the user's actions, ensuring that the assistance provided is both effective and safe. This integration is particularly beneficial in environments where precision and safety are paramount, such as in precision manufacturing and aerospace industries. The ability to adapt to varying task demands and user conditions is a key advantage of these exoskeletons, as they can be tailored to meet the specific needs of different industrial applications [5].

In assistive settings, upper limb exoskeletons serve as critical tools for individuals with motor disabilities, enabling them to perform daily tasks with greater independence. These devices can assist users in tasks such as reaching, grasping, and manipulating objects, which are essential for activities of daily living. The design of assistive exoskeletons often emphasizes comfort, ease of use, and adaptability to different user needs. For example, the development of soft robotic exoskeletons has provided a more natural and comfortable alternative to traditional rigid exoskeletons, allowing for greater range of motion and user compliance [1]. These soft exoskeletons are particularly beneficial for users who require long-term assistance, as they can be worn for extended periods without causing discomfort.

The integration of intelligent control systems and advanced sensing technologies has further enhanced the functionality of assistive exoskeletons. Machine learning algorithms and neural networks are being employed to improve the accuracy of user intent recognition, enabling the exoskeleton to respond more intuitively to the user's movements. For instance, the use of electromyography (EMG) sensors allows the exoskeleton to detect muscle activity and predict the user's intended movements, thereby providing more accurate and timely assistance [43]. This level of intelligence is crucial for assistive applications, as it enables the exoskeleton to adapt to the user's unique needs and preferences, enhancing their overall experience.

Furthermore, the development of exoskeletons that incorporate haptic feedback systems has opened new possibilities for improving user interaction and engagement. Haptic feedback provides users with tactile information, allowing them to sense the force and movement of the exoskeleton, which is essential for tasks requiring precision and control. This feedback mechanism not only enhances the user's sense of control but also contributes to a more natural and intuitive interaction with the exoskeleton. For example, the use of haptic feedback in exoskeletons designed for hand rehabilitation has shown promising results in improving user engagement and promoting functional recovery [44].

In addition to their applications in industrial and assistive contexts, upper limb exoskeletons have also been explored for use in rehabilitation settings, where they play a vital role in aiding patients with motor impairments. The use of exoskeletons in rehabilitation is particularly beneficial for patients recovering from stroke or spinal cord injuries, as they can provide the necessary support and guidance to regain motor function. The integration of advanced control strategies, such as impedance control and model-based control, has enabled exoskeletons to provide more effective and personalized assistance during rehabilitation sessions. For instance, the development of adaptive control systems has allowed exoskeletons to adjust their assistance based on the user's progress, ensuring that the rehabilitation process is both efficient and effective [45].

Moreover, the application of exoskeletons in rehabilitation settings is not limited to physical assistance; they also play a significant role in enhancing the overall user experience. The use of virtual reality (VR) and augmented reality (AR) technologies in conjunction with exoskeletons has created immersive and engaging rehabilitation environments, which can motivate users to participate more actively in their recovery process. These technologies have been shown to improve user engagement and adherence to rehabilitation protocols, ultimately leading to better outcomes [10]. The integration of VR and AR with exoskeletons also allows for the creation of customized rehabilitation programs that can be tailored to the specific needs and goals of each user.

In conclusion, the applications of upper limb exoskeletons in both industrial and assistive contexts are vast and diverse. These devices have the potential to significantly improve worker safety and productivity in industrial settings, while also enhancing the quality of life for individuals with motor disabilities. The integration of advanced sensor technologies, intelligent control systems, and haptic feedback mechanisms has further expanded the capabilities of these exoskeletons, making them more effective and user-friendly. As research in this field continues to advance, the development of more sophisticated and adaptable exoskeletons will likely play an increasingly important role in addressing the challenges faced in both industrial and assistive environments. The ongoing collaboration between researchers, clinicians, and industry experts will be crucial in realizing the full potential of upper limb exoskeletons and ensuring their widespread adoption and effectiveness [38].

### 2.5 Technological Innovations and Trends

Recent technological advancements and trends in upper limb exoskeleton design have significantly transformed the field, making these devices more efficient, user-friendly, and adaptable to a wide range of applications. Among the most notable innovations are the integration of soft robotics, the incorporation of artificial intelligence (AI), and the enhancement of sensor technologies, all of which contribute to more intuitive and responsive exoskeleton systems.

One of the most significant trends in upper limb exoskeleton design is the use of soft robotics. Soft robotics offers a more natural and comfortable interaction with the human body compared to traditional rigid exoskeletons. These systems utilize flexible materials and actuators that can conform to the user’s anatomy, reducing the risk of injury and improving user compliance. The concept of soft robotics has been explored in several studies, highlighting the potential for creating exoskeletons that are not only more comfortable but also capable of performing complex tasks with greater precision. For example, the use of fabric-based pneumatic actuators has been shown to provide controlled force and motion, making them ideal for applications such as infant rehabilitation [46]. Additionally, soft exoskeletons can be designed with a higher degree of adaptability, allowing them to accommodate a wide range of body sizes and movement patterns, which is particularly important for personalized rehabilitation and assistance.

Another critical trend in upper limb exoskeleton design is the integration of AI technologies. AI has the potential to significantly enhance the functionality of exoskeletons by enabling real-time adaptation to the user’s movements and intent. Machine learning algorithms can be used to analyze sensor data and predict the user’s desired actions, allowing the exoskeleton to provide more intuitive and responsive assistance. For instance, the use of neural networks in the development of adaptive control systems has been shown to improve the performance of exoskeletons in dynamic environments [46]. Furthermore, AI can be employed to optimize energy efficiency and reduce the cognitive load on the user by automating complex control processes. Recent studies have demonstrated the effectiveness of AI-driven control strategies in improving the overall performance of exoskeletons, particularly in tasks that require precise and adaptive movements [46]. The integration of AI also facilitates the development of more personalized exoskeleton systems, as machine learning algorithms can learn from the user’s behavior and adjust the assistance provided accordingly [46].

Improved sensor technologies have also played a crucial role in the advancement of upper limb exoskeleton design. Modern exoskeletons are equipped with a variety of sensors, including electromyography (EMG) sensors, force sensors, and vision-based systems, which provide real-time feedback for accurate control and interaction with the user. EMG sensors are particularly useful for capturing muscle activity and recognizing user intent, making them essential for the development of more intuitive control systems [46]. Force and tactile sensors are used to provide feedback on grip force, object contact, and surface properties, which is crucial for tasks that require precision and safety [46]. Vision-based systems, on the other hand, enhance the exoskeleton’s ability to perceive its environment, enabling it to recognize objects and gestures, and adapt to changing conditions [46]. The combination of these sensor technologies allows for the creation of more sophisticated and responsive exoskeleton systems that can better support the user in a variety of tasks.

In addition to soft robotics, AI integration, and improved sensor technologies, the use of multimodal sensor fusion has become a key trend in upper limb exoskeleton design. Multimodal sensor fusion involves combining data from multiple sensor modalities to improve the accuracy, reliability, and adaptability of the exoskeleton’s feedback system. This approach allows for a more comprehensive understanding of the user’s movements and environment, leading to more effective and responsive control strategies [46]. For example, the integration of EMG, vision, and force sensor data can provide a more complete picture of the user’s intentions and actions, enabling the exoskeleton to provide more accurate and timely assistance. The use of multimodal sensor fusion has been shown to enhance the performance of exoskeletons in a variety of applications, including rehabilitation and industrial settings [46].

The advancements in upper limb exoskeleton technology are also being driven by the development of more sophisticated control systems. Techniques such as impedance control, model-based control, and adaptive control are being used to improve the performance of exoskeletons in dynamic and unpredictable environments [46]. Impedance control, for instance, allows the exoskeleton to adjust its stiffness and damping properties in response to external forces, making it more adaptable to different tasks and environments. Model-based control strategies use mathematical models of the exoskeleton and user to predict and optimize system behavior, ensuring accurate and reliable control [46]. Adaptive control techniques, on the other hand, enable the exoskeleton to dynamically adjust its control parameters in response to changes in user conditions, environmental factors, and task requirements [46].

Overall, the recent technological innovations and trends in upper limb exoskeleton design have significantly advanced the field, making these devices more capable, user-friendly, and adaptable. The integration of soft robotics, AI, and advanced sensor technologies has enabled the development of more intuitive and responsive exoskeleton systems that can better support users in a variety of applications. As research in this area continues to evolve, we can expect to see even more sophisticated and effective upper limb exoskeletons in the future.

### 2.6 Challenges in Current Designs

Upper limb exoskeletons have shown great potential in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications, but their widespread adoption is hindered by several critical challenges that affect their practical application. These challenges include ergonomic design issues, limited adaptability to individual user needs, and comfort concerns, all of which significantly impact the effectiveness and usability of these devices.

One of the most prominent challenges in current upper limb exoskeleton designs is the issue of ergonomics. Ergonomic design is crucial for ensuring that the exoskeleton does not cause discomfort or injury to the user during prolonged use. However, many existing exoskeletons are designed with a one-size-fits-all approach, which fails to account for the wide range of human anatomies and movement patterns. This lack of personalization can lead to improper alignment and increased stress on the user’s joints and muscles, reducing the effectiveness of the exoskeleton and potentially causing harm. For instance, the paper "A Test Bench For Evaluating Exoskeletons For Upper Limb Rehabilitation" highlights the importance of accurate joint torque measurements and range of motion (ROM) in evaluating the effectiveness of exoskeletons. The study found that the average experimental torques were overestimated by 40% compared to simulated torques, indicating the need for more precise and user-specific designs that can accommodate individual variations in anatomy and movement [14].

Another major challenge is the adaptability of exoskeletons to different user needs. Upper limb exoskeletons are often designed for specific tasks or patient populations, limiting their versatility in real-world applications. For example, rehabilitation exoskeletons are typically tailored for stroke patients or individuals with spinal cord injuries, but their effectiveness in other scenarios, such as industrial or daily living tasks, is less clear. The paper "A Systematic Approach For Kinematic Design Of Upper Limb Rehabilitation Exoskeletons" emphasizes the need for a systematic approach to kinematic design to ensure that exoskeletons can accommodate a variety of user needs and movement requirements [21]. Without such a systematic approach, exoskeletons may not be able to provide the necessary support for different activities or adapt to the changing needs of the user over time.

User comfort is another significant challenge that affects the practical application of upper limb exoskeletons. Many exoskeletons are heavy and cumbersome, making them impractical for long-term use. The weight and bulkiness of these devices can also restrict the user's natural movement, reducing their effectiveness in assisting daily tasks. Moreover, the materials used in the construction of exoskeletons can cause discomfort, especially if they are not properly designed to conform to the user's body. The paper "Soft robotic suits: State of the art, core technologies and open challenges" highlights the potential of soft robotic suits as a more comfortable alternative to rigid exoskeletons [47]. Soft robotic suits are lightweight, flexible, and can be worn for extended periods without causing discomfort. However, they still face challenges in terms of durability, force transmission, and control accuracy, which need to be addressed to make them more viable for widespread use.

Additionally, the integration of sensors and control systems in current exoskeleton designs poses challenges in terms of user interface and feedback. Accurate and reliable sensing is essential for the exoskeleton to respond appropriately to the user’s movements and intentions. However, many exoskeletons rely on a limited number of sensors, which may not provide sufficient information to accurately interpret the user’s actions. This can lead to delays or inaccuracies in the exoskeleton’s response, reducing its effectiveness. The paper "Intelligent upper-limb exoskeleton integrated with soft wearable bioelectronics and deep-learning for human intention-driven strength augmentation based on sensory feedback" discusses the importance of integrating soft wearable sensors and deep learning algorithms to improve the accuracy of user intention recognition [2]. The study demonstrates that such an approach can significantly enhance the exoskeleton’s ability to respond to the user’s movements in real-time.

Furthermore, the energy efficiency and power management of upper limb exoskeletons remain a significant challenge. Many exoskeletons require a substantial amount of power to operate, which can limit their portability and usability in real-world settings. The paper "Quasi-Direct Drive Actuation for a Lightweight Hip Exoskeleton with High Backdrivability and High Bandwidth" highlights the importance of developing actuators that are both energy-efficient and capable of providing the necessary force and torque [16]. The study presents a novel actuation method that improves energy efficiency while maintaining high performance, demonstrating the potential for more sustainable and practical exoskeleton designs.

In conclusion, while upper limb exoskeletons have the potential to revolutionize rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications, several challenges must be addressed to ensure their widespread adoption. These challenges include ergonomic design, adaptability to individual user needs, user comfort, sensor integration, and energy efficiency. By addressing these challenges through innovative design and technological advancements, the future of upper limb exoskeletons can be made more practical, effective, and user-friendly.

### 2.7 Emerging Research Directions

Emerging research directions in upper limb exoskeleton design are increasingly focused on improving user interaction, personalization, and adaptability through the integration of advanced control systems, machine learning, and human-centric design principles. These innovations aim to address the limitations of traditional exoskeletons, such as limited adaptability, high user burden, and a lack of intuitive control. The future of upper limb exoskeletons lies in developing systems that can seamlessly interact with users, adapt to their needs in real-time, and enhance the user's physical capabilities while ensuring comfort and safety.

One of the most promising areas of research is the development of more intuitive control systems. Traditional control strategies often rely on pre-defined trajectories or simple feedback mechanisms, which can be restrictive and fail to capture the nuances of human movement. To overcome this, researchers are exploring control paradigms that leverage real-time user intent recognition and adaptive assistance. For example, studies have shown that combining electromyography (EMG) with machine learning techniques allows for more accurate interpretation of user intentions, enabling exoskeletons to provide assistance that aligns with the user's motor intent [2]. This integration of EMG and deep learning not only improves the accuracy of movement prediction but also enhances the user's ability to control the exoskeleton intuitively, leading to a more natural and responsive interaction.

Another significant emerging direction is the development of adaptive assistance strategies. Traditional exoskeletons often provide a fixed level of assistance, which may not be optimal for varying tasks or user conditions. Recent research has focused on creating exoskeletons that can dynamically adjust their assistance based on user feedback, environmental conditions, and task requirements. For instance, the use of variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) has shown promise in creating exoskeletons that can modulate their stiffness to match the user's needs [19]. These actuators enable the exoskeleton to provide a range of assistance, from passive support to active reinforcement, depending on the task and the user's level of effort. This adaptability is crucial for ensuring that the exoskeleton can assist the user effectively across a wide range of activities, from simple daily tasks to complex rehabilitation exercises.

The integration of machine learning for enhanced user interaction is another transformative direction in upper limb exoskeleton research. Machine learning algorithms, particularly deep learning, are being used to improve the exoskeleton's ability to understand and respond to user intentions. These algorithms can learn from large datasets of user movements and muscle activity, allowing the exoskeleton to adapt its control strategy in real-time. For example, the use of long short-term memory (LSTM) networks has shown remarkable success in continuously mapping forearm EMG activity to hand kinematics, enabling more accurate and proportional control of hand prostheses [48]. This approach not only improves the functionality of the exoskeleton but also enhances the user's experience by making the system more responsive and personalized.

In addition to these control and interaction advancements, there is a growing emphasis on the design of exoskeletons that prioritize user comfort and ergonomics. Current exoskeletons often suffer from issues such as weight distribution, pressure points, and limited range of motion, which can lead to user discomfort and reduced usability. Emerging research is exploring the use of soft robotics and compliant mechanisms to create exoskeletons that are more flexible and adaptive to the user's anatomy. For example, the development of fabric-based pneumatic actuators has demonstrated the potential to create lightweight and wearable exosuits that provide assistance while maintaining a high degree of comfort [22]. These soft exoskeletons are not only more comfortable to wear but also allow for greater natural movement, making them ideal for long-term use in rehabilitation and daily living applications.

Furthermore, the integration of multimodal sensing and feedback systems is playing a critical role in enhancing user interaction and improving the exoskeleton's ability to adapt to user needs. By combining data from multiple sensors, such as EMG, force, and vision-based systems, researchers are creating more comprehensive feedback loops that enable the exoskeleton to respond more accurately to user inputs. For example, the use of haptic feedback in exoskeletons has shown promise in providing users with tactile information, which enhances their ability to interact with the environment and perform tasks with greater precision [49]. This multimodal approach not only improves the user's control over the exoskeleton but also enhances the overall user experience by providing a more intuitive and responsive interface.

The future of upper limb exoskeletons also involves the development of more personalized and individualized systems. Traditional exoskeletons are often designed with a one-size-fits-all approach, which may not be suitable for all users. Emerging research is focusing on creating exoskeletons that can be tailored to the specific needs and anatomical characteristics of each user. This includes the use of adaptive control algorithms that can adjust the exoskeleton's behavior based on the user's movement patterns and physical capabilities. For instance, the development of a human-in-the-loop learning and adaptation framework has shown the potential to improve the performance of exoskeletons in various tasks and for different users [50]. This approach allows the exoskeleton to continuously learn and adapt to the user's needs, leading to a more effective and personalized assistance experience.

In conclusion, the emerging research directions in upper limb exoskeleton design are focused on creating more intuitive, adaptive, and user-centric systems. By integrating advanced control strategies, machine learning, and human-centric design principles, researchers are paving the way for exoskeletons that can seamlessly interact with users, provide adaptive assistance, and enhance the user's physical capabilities. These innovations hold great promise for transforming the field of upper limb exoskeletons and enabling their widespread adoption in rehabilitation, industrial, and daily living applications.

## 3 Design Principles and Mechanical Configurations

### 3.1 Mechanical Design Principles

The mechanical design of upper limb exoskeletons plays a critical role in determining their overall performance, user comfort, and functionality. A well-designed exoskeleton must balance multiple factors, including structural rigidity, compliance, and the use of lightweight materials, to ensure that the device is both effective and user-friendly. These principles are fundamental in achieving a harmonious interaction between the device and the human body, allowing for smooth movement while minimizing fatigue and discomfort.

One of the most essential mechanical design principles is the use of lightweight materials. The weight of an exoskeleton directly affects the user's physical burden, especially during prolonged use. A lightweight exoskeleton reduces the energy expenditure required to move the device, thereby improving the user's endurance and overall experience. For instance, the development of a portable elbow exoskeleton using a Twisted Strings Actuator (TSA) demonstrated the potential of lightweight and flexible materials in achieving high performance without compromising portability [40]. This approach not only reduces the weight but also allows for a more natural movement, which is crucial for rehabilitation and daily use.

Structural rigidity is another key principle in the mechanical design of upper limb exoskeletons. While flexibility is important, the exoskeleton must also maintain a certain level of rigidity to provide effective support and assistance. This is particularly crucial in applications where the exoskeleton is meant to aid in tasks that require significant force or precision. For example, the design of a lightweight and backdrivable knee exoskeleton demonstrated the importance of structural rigidity in enabling the device to provide reliable assistance during activities such as walking and squatting [51]. This balance between rigidity and flexibility ensures that the exoskeleton can support the user's movements while still allowing for a natural range of motion.

Compliance is another critical aspect of mechanical design, especially in ensuring user comfort and safety. A compliant exoskeleton allows for some degree of flexibility in its joints, which can help to reduce the risk of injury and improve user experience. Compliance is often achieved through the use of compliant mechanisms or low-impedance designs that allow the exoskeleton to adapt to the user's movements. The design of a spine-inspired continuum soft exoskeleton, for instance, emphasized compliance to reduce multiple types of forces along the human spine, such as the spinae muscle force, shear, and compression force of the lumbar vertebrae [52]. This design not only improves user comfort but also enhances the exoskeleton's ability to provide support without restricting natural movement.

In addition to these principles, the mechanical design of upper limb exoskeletons must also consider the ergonomic aspects of the device. Ergonomic design ensures that the exoskeleton fits comfortably and securely on the user's body, minimizing the risk of discomfort or injury. This is particularly important in rehabilitation settings, where the exoskeleton may be used for extended periods. The design of a soft high force hand exoskeleton for rehabilitation and assistance of spinal cord injury and stroke individuals highlighted the importance of ergonomic design, as the device's lightweight and conformal properties ensured a comfortable experience for wearers [53]. Such designs are essential in promoting user adherence and long-term usability.

Another important consideration in the mechanical design of upper limb exoskeletons is the integration of compliant mechanisms. These mechanisms allow the exoskeleton to adapt to the user's movements, reducing the mechanical resistance and improving the overall interaction between the device and the user. The use of compliant mechanisms is particularly beneficial in applications where the exoskeleton needs to provide assistance during dynamic movements, such as walking or reaching. The design of a quasi-direct drive actuation system for a lightweight hip exoskeleton demonstrated the advantages of compliant mechanisms in achieving high backdrivability and bandwidth, which are essential for smooth and natural movement [16]. This design not only improves the exoskeleton's performance but also enhances user comfort and safety.

The choice of materials also plays a significant role in the mechanical design of upper limb exoskeletons. Lightweight and durable materials are preferred to ensure that the device is both functional and long-lasting. The use of advanced materials, such as carbon fiber composites and titanium alloys, has become increasingly common in exoskeleton design due to their high strength-to-weight ratio and corrosion resistance.
