For example, the development of a soft robotic exoskeleton with integrated elastic band actuators demonstrated the effectiveness of using elastic and fabric-based materials to create a device that is both flexible and durable [8]. These materials not only contribute to the lightweight nature of the exoskeleton but also enhance its adaptability and user comfort.

In summary, the mechanical design principles of upper limb exoskeletons are essential in ensuring that these devices are both effective and user-friendly. The use of lightweight materials, structural rigidity, compliance, and ergonomic design all contribute to the overall performance and usability of the exoskeleton. By incorporating these principles, designers can create exoskeletons that not only provide the necessary support and assistance but also enhance the user's comfort and experience. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, these mechanical design principles will remain at the forefront of innovation and development.

### 3.2 Kinematic Configurations

Kinematic configurations are fundamental in the design of upper limb exoskeletons, as they directly influence the system's ability to replicate natural human movements, provide assistance during tasks, and ensure user comfort. The kinematic design of an exoskeleton involves determining the number of degrees of freedom (DoF), joint alignment, and the overall mechanical structure, all of which play a critical role in the system's functionality and performance. The choice of kinematic configuration depends on the specific application of the exoskeleton, whether it is for rehabilitation, industrial assistance, or daily living support. This subsection explores various kinematic configurations used in upper limb exoskeletons, with a focus on how DoF, joint alignment, and kinematic design impact user movement and task performance.

One of the primary considerations in kinematic design is the number of DoF that the exoskeleton should provide. Upper limb exoskeletons typically require at least 3 to 7 DoF to replicate the complex movements of the human arm, including shoulder flexion/extension, shoulder abduction/adduction, elbow flexion/extension, forearm pronation/supination, and wrist flexion/extension. For example, the CLEVERarm, a rehabilitation exoskeleton, incorporates a 7-DoF configuration to enable precise control of the arm's movement during therapy sessions [38]. The increased DoF allows for greater flexibility in performing tasks and adapting to the user's specific needs, but it also introduces challenges in terms of system complexity and control. Conversely, some exoskeletons are designed with fewer DoF to simplify the mechanical structure and improve portability. For instance, the portable elbow exoskeleton developed using a Twisted Strings Actuator (TSA) focuses on providing assistance for elbow flexion and extension, which are critical movements for daily tasks [40]. This trade-off between DoF and simplicity highlights the importance of tailoring the kinematic design to the intended application.

Another key aspect of kinematic design is the alignment of joints in the exoskeleton with the user's anatomical joints. Proper joint alignment ensures that the exoskeleton can effectively transmit forces and torques without causing discomfort or reducing the user's range of motion. Misalignment can lead to mechanical resistance, reduced user compliance, and even injury. For example, the study on the design of a spiral-cable forearm exoskeleton for supination assistance emphasizes the importance of aligning the exoskeleton's joint mechanisms with the user's elbow and wrist to ensure smooth and natural movement [33]. Similarly, the use of soft robotic actuators in hand exoskeletons requires careful alignment to ensure that the actuators can provide assistance without restricting the user's natural hand movements [53]. The alignment of joints in the exoskeleton must also account for variations in user anatomy, which is a challenge for mass-produced devices. Modular and adjustable designs, such as those seen in the M-BLUE (Modular Backdrivable Lower-limb Unloading Exoskeleton), can help address this issue by allowing for customization based on individual user needs [54].

The impact of kinematic design on user movement and task performance is another critical consideration. The kinematic configuration of an exoskeleton determines how well it can assist the user in performing specific tasks, such as grasping objects, reaching for items, or maintaining balance. For instance, the use of a 7-DoF haptic exoskeleton in contact-rich tasks, such as grasping and manipulating objects, requires a kinematic design that can accurately track the user's movements and provide appropriate haptic feedback [15]. On the other hand, exoskeletons designed for rehabilitation often focus on repetitive and controlled movements to promote neuroplasticity and motor recovery. The systematic approach to kinematic design outlined in the study on upper limb rehabilitation exoskeletons emphasizes the need for a well-defined functional decomposition to ensure that the exoskeleton can effectively support the user's movement patterns and task requirements [21].

In addition to DoF and joint alignment, the overall mechanical structure of the exoskeleton also influences its kinematic performance. Rigid exoskeletons, such as those using traditional motors and linkages, provide precise control and high force output but can be bulky and uncomfortable for long-term use. Soft exoskeletons, on the other hand, offer greater flexibility and comfort but may lack the mechanical stiffness required for certain tasks. For example, the fabric-based pneumatic actuators used in soft exosuits provide a lightweight and wearable solution for upper limb assistance, but their compliance can affect the precision of movement control [1]. The choice between rigid and soft mechanisms depends on the specific application, with rigid designs often preferred for industrial and rehabilitation settings, while soft designs are more suitable for daily living support and user-friendly applications.

Furthermore, the kinematic design of an exoskeleton must also consider the user's physical capabilities and limitations. For example, the use of variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) in exoskeletons allows for dynamic adjustments in stiffness, which can improve the user's ability to perform tasks with varying levels of effort [19]. This adaptability is particularly important for users with motor impairments who may require different levels of assistance depending on the task. Similarly, the integration of multimodal sensing and adaptive control strategies can enhance the exoskeleton's ability to respond to the user's movements and adjust the kinematic configuration in real time [55].

In conclusion, the kinematic configurations of upper limb exoskeletons play a critical role in determining their functionality, performance, and usability. The number of DoF, joint alignment, and mechanical structure all influence the exoskeleton's ability to assist users in performing daily tasks, provide rehabilitation support, and maintain user comfort. By carefully considering these factors, researchers and engineers can develop exoskeletons that are both effective and user-friendly, ultimately enhancing the quality of life for individuals with motor impairments.

### 3.3 Actuator Integration and Placement

Actuator integration and placement are critical aspects of upper limb exoskeleton design, as they directly influence the system's performance, mechanical efficiency, and user comfort. The placement of actuators must balance the need for effective force transmission with the requirement to minimize weight and bulk, ensuring that the exoskeleton remains wearable and does not impede natural human movement. Furthermore, the integration of actuators must consider the interaction with human joint dynamics to ensure smooth and safe operation during rehabilitation, industrial, or assistive tasks.  

One of the primary challenges in actuator integration is optimizing mechanical efficiency. Actuators must deliver sufficient force and torque to assist with upper limb movements while minimizing energy consumption and mechanical losses. For example, series elastic actuators (SEAs) have been widely used in exoskeletons due to their ability to provide compliance and reduce motor energy consumption through the use of elastic elements [16]. The torque-elongation profile of these elastic elements plays a critical role in determining the actuator's performance, and convex optimization techniques have been employed to design nonlinear elastic elements that enhance efficiency [16]. Similarly, pneumatic actuators, such as pneumatic artificial muscles (PAMs), offer high power-to-weight ratios and compliance, making them suitable for exoskeleton applications where flexibility and lightweight are essential [1]. However, their hysteresis and nonlinear behavior require careful control strategies to ensure accurate force delivery.  

Weight distribution is another crucial factor in actuator placement. The placement of actuators affects the overall mass and inertia of the exoskeleton, which can significantly impact user comfort and mobility. For instance, the integration of high-torque density motors with low-ratio gear mechanisms in a quasi-direct drive (QDD) actuation system has been shown to reduce the weight of the exoskeleton while maintaining high backdrivability and bandwidth [16]. This design choice not only minimizes the burden on the user but also enhances the exoskeleton's responsiveness during dynamic movements. Similarly, the use of fabric-based pneumatic actuators in soft exosuits has demonstrated the potential for lightweight, flexible actuation systems that can be comfortably worn for extended periods [1]. These actuators are often placed on the upper limbs in strategic locations to provide assistance during flexion and extension without adding significant mass to the system.  

The interaction between actuators and human joint dynamics is a key consideration in actuator integration. The exoskeleton must align with the natural movement patterns of the user to avoid discomfort or injury. For example, the use of compliant mechanisms and low-impedance designs has been shown to improve the interaction between the exoskeleton and the user by reducing mechanical resistance and enhancing adaptability [51]. Additionally, the placement of actuators should account for the biomechanics of the upper limb, ensuring that force is delivered in a manner that complements the user's natural movements. For instance, the design of a lightweight, backdrivable knee exoskeleton with a rolling knee joint and double-hinge mechanisms has been shown to reduce misalignment between the robot joint and the user's knee joint, thereby minimizing discomfort and improving the overall performance of the system [51].  

Another important aspect of actuator integration is the trade-off between mechanical complexity and performance. While more complex actuator systems may offer greater force output and control, they often come at the cost of increased weight, reduced portability, and higher manufacturing costs. For example, the use of rigid actuators in traditional exoskeletons can provide precise control but may limit the user's range of motion and increase the risk of user discomfort [23]. In contrast, soft robotic actuators, such as those used in the development of a fabric-based pneumatic exoskeleton for hand rehabilitation, offer a more flexible and user-friendly alternative [1]. These actuators are designed to conform to the user's anatomy and provide assistance during repetitive tasks without causing fatigue or discomfort.  

In addition to mechanical considerations, the placement of actuators must also account for the control strategies employed by the exoskeleton. For instance, the integration of a feedback controller with a series elastic actuator (SEA) has been shown to improve the performance of upper limb exoskeletons by compensating for the nonlinearities and hysteresis inherent in soft actuators [16]. Similarly, the use of impedance control strategies in exoskeletons allows for dynamic adjustments to the actuator's behavior based on the user's movement and interaction with the environment [56]. This type of adaptive control is particularly important in rehabilitation applications, where the exoskeleton must provide assistance while minimizing the risk of injury.  

Moreover, the design of actuator integration must consider the long-term usability and durability of the exoskeleton. For example, the development of a modular and customizable actuator system has been shown to improve the adaptability of exoskeletons to different user needs and environments [23]. This approach allows for easy maintenance and replacement of components, ensuring that the exoskeleton remains functional over time. Additionally, the use of biostable and biocompatible materials in actuator design is essential to ensure user safety and minimize the risk of adverse reactions.  

Finally, the integration of actuators must be guided by the specific application requirements of the exoskeleton. For instance, the design of a portable elbow exoskeleton with a twisted strings actuator (TSA) has been optimized for upper limb rehabilitation, with a focus on lightweight and compact form factors [40]. In industrial settings, exoskeletons must be designed to withstand high workloads and provide consistent assistance over extended periods, which may necessitate the use of more robust and durable actuator systems [57].  

In summary, actuator integration and placement in upper limb exoskeletons require a careful balance of mechanical efficiency, weight distribution, and user interaction. By considering the specific requirements of each application and leveraging advanced actuator technologies, designers can create exoskeletons that are both effective and user-friendly. The insights from various studies on actuator design, control strategies, and user interaction highlight the importance of a holistic approach to actuator integration, ensuring that the exoskeleton enhances human performance without compromising comfort or safety.

### 3.4 Structural Considerations for User Fit and Adaptability

The design of upper limb exoskeletons requires a strong focus on structural considerations to ensure proper user fit and adaptability. These exoskeletons must be designed to accommodate a wide range of user sizes, anatomical variations, and individual preferences, while maintaining comfort, functionality, and safety. Achieving this requires a combination of adjustable components, ergonomic design, and adaptive mechanisms that allow the exoskeleton to respond to different user needs and movements.

One of the primary challenges in achieving proper user fit is the variability in human anatomy. Upper limb exoskeletons must be able to accommodate differences in arm length, joint alignment, and body proportions. To address this, many exoskeleton designs incorporate adjustable components that can be customized to fit individual users. For example, the use of modular and adjustable straps, hinges, and joints allows the exoskeleton to adapt to different body dimensions and movement patterns [14]. These adjustable features not only improve user comfort but also ensure that the exoskeleton does not impede natural movement or cause discomfort during prolonged use.

Ergonomic design plays a critical role in ensuring that upper limb exoskeletons are both functional and comfortable. Ergonomic considerations include the placement of sensors, actuators, and control systems to minimize interference with the user’s natural movements. For instance, the placement of sensors on the exoskeleton should be designed to capture accurate biomechanical data without restricting the user’s range of motion or causing discomfort [14]. Similarly, the design of the exoskeleton's structural framework must be optimized to distribute weight evenly and reduce pressure points, which can significantly improve user comfort and reduce the risk of injury.

Another important structural consideration is the ability of the exoskeleton to adapt to different user sizes and anatomical variations. This can be achieved through the use of adjustable joints, modular components, and customizable fit options. For example, some exoskeletons use adjustable joint configurations that allow users to fine-tune the range of motion and alignment of the exoskeleton to match their specific needs [14]. This level of customization is essential for ensuring that the exoskeleton functions effectively across a diverse population of users, including those with different body types, mobility limitations, and physical impairments.

The integration of adaptive control systems also plays a key role in improving user fit and adaptability. These systems allow the exoskeleton to dynamically adjust its support and assistance based on the user's movements and needs. For example, some exoskeletons use real-time feedback from sensors to adjust the level of assistance provided, ensuring that the user receives optimal support without overexertion or discomfort [14]. This adaptability not only enhances user comfort but also improves the effectiveness of the exoskeleton in performing its intended tasks.

In addition to adjustable components and adaptive control systems, the material selection of the exoskeleton also has a significant impact on user fit and comfort. Lightweight and flexible materials are often used to reduce the overall weight of the exoskeleton and improve its wearability. For instance, soft robotic exoskeletons made from fabric-based actuators and flexible polymers are designed to provide a more natural and unobtrusive fit compared to rigid, traditional exoskeletons [8]. These materials also allow for greater flexibility and compliance, which can enhance the user's range of motion and reduce the risk of injury.

The structural design of the exoskeleton must also consider the interaction between the device and the user’s body. This includes ensuring that the exoskeleton does not impose unnecessary constraints on the user’s natural movements. For example, the use of compliant mechanisms and low-impedance designs allows the exoskeleton to move in harmony with the user’s body, reducing the risk of mechanical resistance and discomfort [14]. These designs are particularly important for exoskeletons used in rehabilitation and assistance applications, where the user’s comfort and mobility are critical to the success of the intervention.

Another key consideration is the ease of donning and doffing the exoskeleton. A well-designed exoskeleton should allow users to quickly and easily put it on and take it off without requiring extensive assistance. This is particularly important for users who may have limited mobility or dexterity, such as individuals with motor impairments [14]. Features such as quick-release buckles, adjustable straps, and intuitive fastening mechanisms can significantly improve the user experience and promote consistent use of the exoskeleton.

The ability of the exoskeleton to adapt to different tasks and environments is also a critical structural consideration. Exoskeletons used in industrial and assistive applications must be able to perform a wide range of movements and tasks, which requires the exoskeleton to be both robust and adaptable. For example, the design of the exoskeleton’s joints and actuators must allow for a high degree of flexibility and range of motion, enabling the user to perform complex movements with ease [14]. This adaptability is essential for ensuring that the exoskeleton can effectively support users in a variety of tasks and environments.

In summary, the structural considerations for user fit and adaptability in upper limb exoskeleton design are multifaceted and require a combination of adjustable components, ergonomic design, and adaptive mechanisms. By addressing these considerations, exoskeletons can be made more comfortable, effective, and user-friendly, thereby enhancing their usability in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, the focus on user-centric design will remain a critical factor in the success of these devices.

### 3.5 Compliance and Backdrivability

Compliance and backdrivability are critical design considerations in upper limb exoskeletons, as they significantly influence user interaction, comfort, and overall system performance. Compliance refers to the ability of a system to yield or flex in response to external forces, while backdrivability denotes the capacity of a system to be moved by external forces rather than solely by its internal actuators. Together, these properties enable more natural and intuitive human-robot interaction, reducing mechanical resistance and enhancing user safety. This subsection explores the role of compliance and backdrivability in upper limb exoskeletons, emphasizing their importance in the design of compliant mechanisms and low-impedance systems.

Compliant mechanisms are designed to allow for controlled deformation in response to external forces, which is essential for ensuring that the exoskeleton does not impose rigid constraints on user movement. This is particularly important in upper limb exoskeletons, where the human arm is capable of complex and multi-degree-of-freedom movements. By incorporating compliant elements, such as springs, flexible joints, or soft materials, exoskeletons can adapt to the user’s natural motion, reducing the risk of mechanical resistance and increasing user comfort. For instance, compliant designs can help mitigate the risk of user injury by allowing the exoskeleton to yield when encountering unexpected forces, thus reducing the mechanical stress on the user’s joints and muscles. This is particularly beneficial in rehabilitation settings, where the user’s physical condition may vary, and the exoskeleton must be able to accommodate a wide range of movements and forces.

Low-impedance designs further contribute to enhancing user interaction by minimizing the mechanical resistance between the user and the exoskeleton. Impedance, in this context, refers to the system’s resistance to motion, and a low-impedance design ensures that the exoskeleton does not hinder the user’s natural movement. This is achieved through the use of actuators and control strategies that allow the exoskeleton to adaptively adjust its force output based on the user’s movements. For example, impedance control can be used to modulate the exoskeleton’s resistance in real-time, ensuring that it provides assistance only when necessary and does not impede the user’s motion. This approach is particularly useful in applications where the user needs to perform precise and delicate tasks, such as in industrial settings or during rehabilitation exercises.

The integration of compliant mechanisms and low-impedance designs is a key aspect of modern upper limb exoskeleton research. For instance, recent studies have explored the use of soft robotics and variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) to achieve greater compliance and backdrivability. Soft robotic exoskeletons, which are made from flexible and lightweight materials, can conform to the user’s body and provide assistance without imposing rigid constraints on movement [8]. These exoskeletons can be designed to have a high degree of compliance, allowing them to adapt to the user’s movements and reduce mechanical resistance. Additionally, the use of VSAs enables the exoskeleton to modulate its stiffness in real-time, further enhancing its ability to adapt to the user’s needs and improve overall performance.

Another critical aspect of compliance and backdrivability is the role of control systems in ensuring that the exoskeleton interacts safely and effectively with the user. Advanced control strategies, such as impedance control and model predictive control (MPC), are often employed to achieve a balance between assistance and user autonomy. Impedance control, for example, allows the exoskeleton to adjust its resistance based on the user’s movements, ensuring that it provides assistance without impeding the user’s natural motion. This is particularly important in rehabilitation settings, where the user may need to perform tasks that require precise and controlled movements. By incorporating impedance control, exoskeletons can provide the necessary support while also allowing the user to maintain a sense of agency and control over their movements.

Moreover, the use of compliant mechanisms and low-impedance designs can significantly enhance the usability and acceptance of upper limb exoskeletons. Users are more likely to accept and use an exoskeleton that feels natural and intuitive, as opposed to one that imposes rigid constraints and mechanical resistance. This is particularly relevant in assistive and industrial applications, where the exoskeleton must be worn for extended periods and must not cause discomfort or fatigue. By designing exoskeletons with compliance and backdrivability in mind, developers can create systems that are more user-friendly and adaptable to different user needs.

In addition to improving user interaction, compliance and backdrivability also play a crucial role in ensuring the safety and reliability of upper limb exoskeletons. Compliant mechanisms can help prevent accidents by allowing the exoskeleton to yield when encountering unexpected forces, reducing the risk of injury to the user. This is particularly important in environments where the user may be exposed to unpredictable conditions, such as in industrial settings or during rehabilitation exercises. Furthermore, low-impedance designs can help reduce the risk of mechanical failure by minimizing the stress on the exoskeleton’s components, thereby improving its overall durability and reliability.

In conclusion, compliance and backdrivability are essential design principles in upper limb exoskeletons, as they significantly influence user interaction, comfort, and system performance. By incorporating compliant mechanisms and low-impedance designs, exoskeletons can adapt to the user’s natural movements, reduce mechanical resistance, and enhance safety and usability. The integration of advanced control strategies, such as impedance control and MPC, further enhances the ability of exoskeletons to provide effective and intuitive assistance. As research in this field continues to evolve, the development of more compliant and backdrivable exoskeletons will play a vital role in expanding the applications of upper limb exoskeletons in rehabilitation, industry, and daily life [8; 45].

### 3.6 Load Distribution and Mechanical Efficiency

Load distribution and mechanical efficiency are fundamental principles in the design of upper limb exoskeletons, as they directly influence the performance, energy consumption, and overall usability of the system. Proper load distribution ensures that the forces exerted by the exoskeleton are evenly spread across the user's limbs and joints, reducing the risk of localized stress and discomfort. Mechanical efficiency, on the other hand, refers to the ability of the exoskeleton to transmit forces effectively while minimizing energy loss. Optimizing these aspects is critical for ensuring that the exoskeleton operates smoothly and sustainably, particularly in long-term rehabilitation or industrial applications.

One of the key challenges in load distribution is ensuring that the exoskeleton's actuators and structural components are positioned in a way that maximizes their effectiveness. For instance, if the actuators are placed too far from the joints they are meant to assist, the mechanical advantage may be reduced, leading to inefficient force transmission. In contrast, placing actuators closer to the joints can improve the mechanical efficiency of the system by reducing the moment arm and allowing for more direct force application. This principle is particularly relevant in the design of exoskeletons for the elbow, where the location of the actuators can significantly affect the user's ability to perform tasks such as lifting or reaching.

The importance of load distribution is highlighted in the design of the portable elbow exoskeleton developed using a Twisted Strings Actuator (TSA) [40]. The researchers optimized the actuator's placement to ensure that the force applied to the elbow joint was distributed evenly, thereby reducing the risk of localized pressure points and improving user comfort. Similarly, the design of the knee exoskeleton in the study [12] emphasized the importance of load distribution by incorporating compliant actuation, which allowed the exoskeleton to absorb and distribute forces more effectively during dynamic movements. These examples demonstrate how careful attention to load distribution can enhance both the comfort and functionality of upper limb exoskeletons.

Mechanical efficiency is closely tied to the choice of actuation systems and the overall mechanical design of the exoskeleton. For example, pneumatic actuators, such as those used in the fabric-based pneumatic exosuit [58], are known for their high power-to-weight ratio and inherent compliance, making them ideal for applications where energy efficiency is a priority. However, the efficiency of these systems can be further improved by optimizing the design of the actuator's components, such as the fabric-based chambers and the way they are integrated into the exoskeleton's structure. In this regard, the concept of "Volume Transfer" introduced in [59] represents a significant advancement, as it allows for the redistribution of the actuator's volume to enhance both the mechanical performance and the wearability of the exosuit.

Another aspect of mechanical efficiency is the minimization of energy loss during force transmission. This can be achieved through the use of low-impedance mechanical designs, which allow for smooth and efficient transfer of forces between the exoskeleton and the user's body. The study [51] highlights the importance of backdrivability in reducing mechanical resistance and ensuring that the exoskeleton does not impede the user's natural movements. By using low-impedance transmissions and compliant mechanisms, the exoskeleton can operate with minimal energy consumption while still providing the necessary assistance.

In addition to actuation and structural design, the mechanical efficiency of an exoskeleton is also influenced by the way it interacts with the user's musculoskeletal system. For instance, the use of adaptive control strategies can help the exoskeleton adjust its force output in real-time, ensuring that the user is not over-assisted or under-assisted. This is particularly important in rehabilitation settings, where the exoskeleton must provide the right amount of assistance to support the user's recovery without hindering their natural movement patterns. The study [17] demonstrates how model predictive control (MPC) can be used to optimize the exoskeleton's assistance based on the user's movements and muscle activity, leading to more efficient and effective assistance.

Furthermore, the efficiency of the exoskeleton can be improved by reducing the weight of the system and minimizing the number of mechanical components. Lighter exoskeletons require less energy to operate and are more comfortable for the user to wear for extended periods. The design of the modular backdrivable lower-limb exoskeleton [54] exemplifies this approach, as it uses high-torque, low-impedance actuators to provide assistance while keeping the overall weight of the device to a minimum. This design not only improves mechanical efficiency but also enhances the user's mobility and comfort.

In summary, load distribution and mechanical efficiency are critical factors in the design of upper limb exoskeletons, as they directly impact the system's performance, energy consumption, and user experience. By optimizing the placement of actuators, reducing mechanical resistance, and minimizing energy loss, designers can create exoskeletons that are both effective and efficient. The examples provided in various studies demonstrate the importance of these principles in the development of advanced exoskeleton technologies, paving the way for more widespread adoption in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications.

### 3.7 Material Selection and Durability

The selection of materials for upper limb exoskeletons is a critical design consideration that directly impacts their performance, user safety, and long-term usability. The choice of materials must balance several key factors, including strength, weight, durability, and biocompatibility. These factors are interrelated and must be optimized to meet the specific requirements of the exoskeleton's intended application, whether it be rehabilitation, industrial assistance, or daily living support. 

Strength is a fundamental requirement for upper limb exoskeletons, as they must withstand the forces exerted by the user during movement and interaction with the environment. The materials used must be able to support the weight of the exoskeleton and any external loads applied during tasks such as lifting or grasping. In this context, materials like carbon fiber composites and aluminum alloys are often utilized due to their high strength-to-weight ratio. For instance, the design of a lightweight and backdrivable knee exoskeleton for mobility assistance of children with crouch gait incorporates high-strength, lightweight materials to ensure durability while minimizing the burden on the user [31]. These materials allow the exoskeleton to maintain structural integrity under varying load conditions, ensuring reliable performance over extended periods.

Weight is another critical factor, as excessive weight can lead to user fatigue and discomfort, particularly during prolonged use. Lightweight materials such as carbon fiber and titanium alloys are often chosen to reduce the overall weight of the exoskeleton without compromising structural integrity. The development of a portable elbow exoskeleton using a Twisted Strings Actuator (TSA) exemplifies this approach, where the use of lightweight materials contributes to the device's portability and ease of use [40]. By minimizing the weight of the exoskeleton, users can engage in activities for longer durations without experiencing excessive fatigue, which is particularly important in rehabilitation and daily living scenarios.

Durability is essential for ensuring the long-term usability of upper limb exoskeletons. The materials used must be resistant to wear and tear, capable of withstanding repeated use, and able to maintain their structural properties over time. In this regard, the use of high-performance polymers and advanced composites is often employed to enhance the longevity of the exoskeleton. For example, the design of a soft robotic exoskeleton for hand rehabilitation incorporates flexible, durable materials that can withstand the stresses of repeated movement while maintaining comfort and functionality [1]. These materials are engineered to be resilient, ensuring that the exoskeleton remains effective and reliable throughout its operational lifespan.

Biocompatibility is a crucial consideration for upper limb exoskeletons, especially those that come into direct contact with the user's skin. The materials used must be non-toxic, hypoallergenic, and comfortable to wear for extended periods. The development of a fabric-based pneumatic actuator for exosuits highlights the importance of biocompatibility, as the materials used are designed to be flexible, breathable, and gentle on the skin [60]. By prioritizing biocompatibility, designers can ensure that the exoskeleton does not cause irritation or discomfort, which is essential for maintaining user compliance and satisfaction.

In addition to these primary factors, the selection of materials must also consider the environmental impact and sustainability of the exoskeleton. The use of eco-friendly and recyclable materials can reduce the environmental footprint of the exoskeleton and align with broader sustainability goals. For example, the development of a lightweight and backdrivable knee exoskeleton for mobility assistance of children with crouch gait incorporates materials that are not only lightweight but also environmentally friendly [31]. This approach not only enhances the performance of the exoskeleton but also contributes to a more sustainable future.

The integration of advanced materials in upper limb exoskeletons also enables the development of more sophisticated and adaptive systems. For instance, the use of shape-memory alloys (SMAs) in exoskeletons allows for the creation of high-force, high-displacement actuators that can be controlled with precision [61]. These materials offer the potential for creating exoskeletons that can dynamically adjust their properties in response to the user's needs, enhancing the overall performance and adaptability of the device.

Moreover, the selection of materials must also take into account the specific requirements of different applications. For example, in rehabilitation settings, the exoskeleton must be designed to provide support and assistance without causing any additional strain on the user. The use of soft, compliant materials in the design of an upper limb exoskeleton for rehabilitation demonstrates how material selection can be tailored to meet the specific needs of the application [1]. These materials allow the exoskeleton to move in harmony with the user, providing a more natural and intuitive experience.

In industrial applications, where the exoskeleton may be subjected to more rigorous conditions, the selection of materials must prioritize robustness and resistance to wear. The development of a cable-driven exoskeleton for industrial use highlights the importance of durability, as the materials used must withstand the repeated stresses of industrial tasks while maintaining their structural integrity [57]. This approach ensures that the exoskeleton can perform reliably in demanding environments, reducing the risk of failure and enhancing overall safety.

In conclusion, the selection of materials for upper limb exoskeletons is a multifaceted process that requires careful consideration of strength, weight, durability, and biocompatibility. By choosing materials that meet these criteria, designers can create exoskeletons that are not only functional and reliable but also comfortable and safe for long-term use. The integration of advanced materials and innovative design approaches is essential for the continued evolution of upper limb exoskeletons, enabling them to meet the diverse needs of users in various applications.

### 3.8 Modular and Customizable Design Approaches

[32]

Modular and customizable design approaches in upper limb exoskeletons have emerged as a critical paradigm in the development of wearable assistive and rehabilitative technologies. These approaches prioritize adaptability, ease of maintenance, and personalization to address the diverse needs of users and the dynamic requirements of different tasks. The increasing recognition of the need for personalized and user-centric designs has driven researchers to explore modular systems that can be tailored to specific anatomical and functional requirements, as well as to the varying conditions of users.

One of the primary benefits of modular design in upper limb exoskeletons is the ability to adapt the device to the user's anatomical structure. Traditional exoskeletons often suffer from a one-size-fits-all approach, which can lead to discomfort, reduced usability, and even injury due to poor fit [8]. Modular systems, on the other hand, allow for the interchangeability of components, enabling adjustments to the exoskeleton's structure and actuation mechanisms to accommodate different body sizes and shapes. This adaptability is particularly crucial in rehabilitation settings, where patients may have varying levels of mobility and different degrees of joint impairment. For instance, the modular backdrivable lower-limb unloading exoskeleton (M-BLUE) demonstrates the potential of modular design to address the heterogeneous needs of individuals with mobility disabilities [54]. The M-BLUE's design allows for the configuration of different hip and knee assistance setups, thereby enabling the device to be customized for individual user requirements.

Customizability also plays a significant role in the maintenance and longevity of upper limb exoskeletons. By designing systems with modular components, manufacturers can facilitate easier repairs and upgrades without requiring complete replacement of the device. This not only reduces the cost of ownership but also extends the lifespan of the exoskeleton. For example, the use of soft, fabric-based pneumatic actuators in exosuits allows for a high degree of flexibility and wearability, while also enabling the integration of modular components that can be individually replaced or upgraded as needed [8]. This kind of design approach is especially beneficial in industrial applications, where exoskeletons may be subjected to heavy use and require frequent maintenance. Modular systems also enable the incorporation of advanced sensors and actuators in specific parts of the exoskeleton, thereby improving the device's overall performance and responsiveness to user needs [5].

Personalization is another key aspect of modular and customizable design in upper limb exoskeletons. The ability to tailor the exoskeleton to the user's specific tasks and movement patterns enhances the effectiveness of the device in both rehabilitation and daily activities. For instance, the use of intelligent control systems that integrate soft wearable bioelectronics and deep-learning algorithms allows for the prediction of user intent and the customization of assistance strategies based on individual needs [2]. These systems can learn from the user's movement patterns and adjust the level of assistance in real-time, thereby improving the user experience and ensuring that the exoskeleton provides the most appropriate support for the task at hand.

In addition to individual customization, modular design also facilitates the adaptation of exoskeletons to different environments and tasks. For example, the development of portable exoskeletons, such as the one described in the paper "The development of a portable elbow exoskeleton with a Twisted Strings Actuator to assist patients with upper limb inhabitation," demonstrates how modular components can be used to create compact and lightweight devices that are suitable for use in various settings [40]. These devices can be easily transported and adapted to different therapeutic or industrial applications, thereby expanding their utility and accessibility.

Moreover, the integration of modular and customizable design approaches into upper limb exoskeletons has the potential to significantly reduce the cost of development and deployment. By standardizing certain components and allowing for the reuse of design elements across different models, manufacturers can streamline production processes and reduce the time and resources required to develop new exoskeletons. This is particularly important in the context of healthcare, where cost-effectiveness is a key consideration for both providers and users. The design of generic hand exoskeletons that are application-agnostic is a prime example of how modular approaches can be used to create cost-effective solutions that can be adapted to a wide range of rehabilitation and assistive needs [7].

The benefits of modular and customizable design approaches are further underscored by the need for adaptability in the face of changing user conditions and requirements. As users progress in their rehabilitation or as their needs evolve, the ability to modify and reconfigure the exoskeleton ensures that the device remains relevant and effective over time. For example, the use of adaptive control strategies in exoskeletons that can dynamically adjust to changes in user conditions or task demands is a critical feature of modern exoskeleton design [26]. These strategies, when combined with modular components, enable the exoskeleton to provide personalized and responsive assistance, thereby enhancing the user's experience and the device's overall effectiveness.

In conclusion, the adoption of modular and customizable design approaches in upper limb exoskeletons represents a significant advancement in the field of wearable assistive technologies. By emphasizing adaptability, ease of maintenance, and personalization, these approaches address the diverse needs of users and the dynamic requirements of different tasks. The integration of modular components, the use of intelligent control systems, and the focus on user-centric design all contribute to the development of more effective, efficient, and accessible exoskeletons. As research in this area continues to evolve, the potential for further innovations in modular and customizable design will likely play a crucial role in shaping the future of upper limb exoskeleton technology.

## 4 Actuator and Drive Mechanisms

### 4.1 Series Elastic Actuators (SEAs)

Series Elastic Actuators (SEAs) have emerged as a pivotal innovation in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design, offering a unique blend of energy efficiency, compliance, and adaptability. The fundamental principle of SEAs lies in the incorporation of an elastic element between the motor and the load, which serves to decouple the motor's dynamics from the external forces. This design allows for the reduction of motor energy consumption and peak power, which are critical factors in the performance of exoskeleton systems, especially in applications where energy efficiency and long operational durations are paramount [62].

The importance of the torque-elongation profile of the elastic element in SEAs cannot be overstated. This profile determines the relationship between the torque applied to the actuator and the resulting elongation of the elastic component. A well-designed torque-elongation profile ensures that the actuator can provide the necessary force while maintaining compliance, which is essential for safe and effective human-robot interaction. In the context of upper limb exoskeletons, this compliance is crucial for reducing the risk of injury and enhancing user comfort [62].

The use of convex optimization in the design of nonlinear elastic elements further enhances the performance of SEAs. Convex optimization techniques allow for the efficient exploration of the design space, ensuring that the elastic element is optimized for specific applications. This approach enables the design of SEAs that can adapt to varying load conditions, thereby improving the overall efficiency and effectiveness of the exoskeleton system. For instance, in the development of a lightweight hip exoskeleton, convex optimization was employed to fine-tune the actuator's characteristics, resulting in a significant improvement in backdrivability and control bandwidth [16].

One of the key advantages of SEAs is their ability to reduce the peak power required by the motor. By introducing an elastic element, SEAs can store and release energy in a controlled manner, which helps in smoothing out the power demands on the motor. This is particularly beneficial in scenarios where the exoskeleton needs to perform repetitive or high-impact movements, as it reduces the risk of motor overheating and extends the lifespan of the actuator [62]. For example, in the context of a portable elbow exoskeleton, the integration of SEAs allowed for the efficient transmission of torque, enabling the device to provide assistance without excessive energy consumption [40].

Another significant benefit of SEAs is their role in improving the energy efficiency of exoskeleton systems. By decoupling the motor from the load, SEAs can reduce the amount of energy wasted in the form of heat and mechanical losses. This is particularly important in applications where the exoskeleton needs to operate for extended periods, such as in rehabilitation settings or industrial environments. The energy efficiency of SEAs is further enhanced by their ability to store and release energy, which can be leveraged to reduce the overall power consumption of the system. For instance, in the development of a knee exoskeleton, the use of SEAs enabled the device to provide assistance during walking without requiring continuous high-power input from the motor [31].

The compliance provided by SEAs also plays a crucial role in ensuring safe and effective human-robot interaction. By allowing the actuator to yield to external forces, SEAs can reduce the risk of injury and improve the overall safety of the exoskeleton system. This is particularly important in rehabilitation applications, where the exoskeleton needs to work in close proximity to the user's body. The compliance of SEAs also enhances the user's comfort by reducing the mechanical resistance encountered during movement. For example, in the design of a soft hand exoskeleton, the use of SEAs allowed for a more natural and comfortable interaction between the device and the user's hand [8].

In addition to their mechanical advantages, SEAs also offer significant benefits in terms of control and adaptability. The decoupling of the motor from the load allows for more precise control of the actuator's output, which is essential for tasks that require high accuracy and responsiveness. The compliance of SEAs also enables the exoskeleton to adapt to varying user conditions, making it more versatile and user-friendly. For instance, in the development of a hip exoskeleton, the use of SEAs allowed for the creation of a system that could provide assistance during different types of movements, such as walking, sitting, and standing [16].

The design of SEAs also involves careful consideration of the elastic element's material and geometry. The choice of materials and the design of the elastic element can significantly impact the performance of the actuator. For example, the use of high-performance polymers and composites can enhance the durability and energy efficiency of the actuator, while the geometry of the elastic element can influence the torque-elongation profile and the overall compliance of the system. In the context of upper limb exoskeletons, the design of the elastic element is often tailored to match the specific requirements of the application, such as the need for high force output or low mechanical resistance [62].

The integration of SEAs into upper limb exoskeletons also requires a thorough understanding of the human biomechanics and the specific movement patterns of the user. This involves not only the design of the elastic element but also the development of control strategies that can effectively utilize the compliance of the actuator. For example, in the development of a rehabilitation exoskeleton, the control system was designed to take advantage of the compliance of SEAs, allowing the device to provide assistance that is both effective and safe [40].

In summary, Series Elastic Actuators (SEAs) represent a significant advancement in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design, offering a range of benefits that include energy efficiency, compliance, and adaptability. The principles of SEAs, the importance of the torque-elongation profile, and the use of convex optimization for nonlinear elastic element design are all critical factors that contribute to the performance of these actuators. By leveraging the advantages of SEAs, researchers and engineers can develop exoskeleton systems that are not only more efficient and effective but also safer and more comfortable for users [62].

### 4.2 Pneumatic Actuators

Pneumatic actuators have emerged as a promising solution for upper limb exoskeletons due to their unique combination of lightweight, compliance, and high power-to-weight ratio [8]. These attributes make them particularly suitable for applications where user comfort, portability, and energy efficiency are critical, such as in rehabilitation and assistive robotics. Unlike rigid actuators, which are often heavy and inflexible, pneumatic actuators can be designed to provide smooth, natural movements that closely mimic human biomechanics. This is especially important in exoskeletons, where the interaction between the device and the user must be seamless to ensure both effectiveness and safety [7].

One of the primary advantages of pneumatic actuators is their ability to generate high forces relative to their weight. This is achieved through the use of compressed air, which can be rapidly expanded to provide significant mechanical output. This characteristic makes them ideal for applications where force generation is essential, such as in assisting users with weakened musculature during rehabilitation exercises [8]. Additionally, the compliance of pneumatic actuators allows for safe and intuitive human-robot interaction, reducing the risk of injury during dynamic tasks [40].

Despite these benefits, pneumatic actuators also present several challenges, particularly in terms of hysteresis and dynamic control. Hysteresis refers to the lag between the input and output of an actuator, which can lead to reduced precision and responsiveness. This is a common issue in pneumatic systems, where the pressure and flow dynamics are not perfectly linear. The hysteresis effect can be particularly problematic in exoskeleton applications, where precise control is necessary to ensure that the device responds appropriately to user movements and intentions [20].

To address these challenges, researchers have developed various models and control strategies to improve the performance of pneumatic actuators. One of the most widely used models for characterizing hysteresis in pneumatic systems is the Bouc-Wen hysteresis model. This model is known for its ability to capture the nonlinear behavior of pneumatic actuators with high accuracy, making it a valuable tool for both simulation and control design [20]. The Bouc-Wen model is particularly useful in applications where the actuator's response must be predicted and controlled with high precision, such as in robotic systems that require dynamic and adaptive behaviors.

The Bouc-Wen model is based on a set of differential equations that describe the relationship between the actuator's input (e.g., pressure) and output (e.g., force or displacement). It accounts for the hysteresis effect by introducing a nonlinear term that represents the energy dissipation in the system. This model can be incorporated into control algorithms to compensate for the hysteresis effect, thereby improving the accuracy and responsiveness of the actuator. For example, in the context of upper limb exoskeletons, the Bouc-Wen model can be used to design control strategies that adjust the actuator's output in real time, ensuring that the device provides the appropriate level of assistance without introducing unwanted delays or errors [20].

In addition to hysteresis, dynamic control is another critical challenge in the use of pneumatic actuators. The dynamic behavior of these actuators can be highly nonlinear and difficult to predict, which makes it challenging to design control systems that can handle a wide range of operating conditions. To overcome this, researchers have explored various control techniques, including model-based control, adaptive control, and hybrid control strategies. These approaches aim to improve the responsiveness and accuracy of pneumatic actuators by incorporating real-time data and feedback into the control process [20].

One of the key benefits of using the Bouc-Wen model in conjunction with advanced control strategies is that it enables the development of more robust and reliable exoskeleton systems. By accurately modeling the hysteresis and dynamic behavior of the actuators, researchers can design control algorithms that adapt to changes in the user's movements and environmental conditions. This is particularly important in rehabilitation applications, where the exoskeleton must be able to provide consistent and effective assistance over a wide range of tasks and user conditions [20].

In conclusion, pneumatic actuators offer a compelling solution for upper limb exoskeletons due to their lightweight, compliance, and high power-to-weight ratio. However, their performance is often limited by challenges such as hysteresis and dynamic control. The use of the Bouc-Wen hysteresis model and advanced control strategies can help to overcome these challenges, leading to more accurate, responsive, and user-friendly exoskeleton systems. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, the integration of pneumatic actuators and sophisticated control methods will play a crucial role in the development of next-generation devices that can provide effective and safe assistance to users with a wide range of needs.

### 4.3 Shape-Memory Alloys (SMAs)

Shape-memory alloys (SMAs) have gained significant attention in the field of exoskeleton design due to their unique ability to generate high forces and displacements in response to temperature changes. SMAs, such as nickel-titanium (NiTi) alloys, can undergo a phase transformation when heated or cooled, allowing them to return to a predetermined shape. This property makes them an attractive option for actuation in exoskeletons, as they can provide the necessary force and motion without the need for complex mechanical systems. However, the integration of SMAs into exoskeletons presents several challenges that must be addressed to ensure reliable and consistent performance.

One of the primary advantages of SMAs is their ability to generate high forces relative to their size and weight. This makes them particularly suitable for applications where space and weight are critical constraints, such as in upper limb exoskeletons. For instance, the use of SMA actuators in exoskeletons can enable the creation of lightweight and compact devices that can assist users in performing daily tasks. However, the force output of SMAs is highly dependent on temperature, which can lead to inconsistencies in performance. The phase transformation in SMAs is a thermally driven process, and the temperature at which this transformation occurs can vary depending on the material properties and the surrounding environment. This variability can result in unpredictable force outputs, which can be a significant challenge in ensuring the reliability of SMA-based exoskeletons.

To address the issue of inconsistent force output, researchers have explored various methods to control the temperature of SMAs. One approach is the integration of temperature control systems that can monitor and adjust the temperature of the SMA actuators in real-time. These systems typically use feedback mechanisms to regulate the temperature, ensuring that the SMA operates within the desired range. For example, the use of thermistors or other temperature sensors can provide real-time data on the temperature of the SMA, allowing for precise control. However, the design and implementation of such temperature control systems can be complex and may add to the overall cost and weight of the exoskeleton. Additionally, the response time of the temperature control system can affect the performance of the exoskeleton, particularly in dynamic tasks where rapid adjustments are required.

Another challenge associated with the use of SMAs in exoskeletons is the need for reliable and efficient power sources. The phase transformation in SMAs requires energy input, typically in the form of electrical current, to initiate the transformation. This energy requirement can be significant, especially when multiple SMA actuators are used in a single exoskeleton. The power consumption of SMA actuators can also vary depending on the desired force output and the operating conditions, making it essential to optimize the energy efficiency of the system. Researchers have explored various power management strategies to address this challenge, including the use of energy recovery systems and advanced control algorithms that minimize energy consumption while maintaining performance.

The integration of SMAs into exoskeletons also raises concerns about the durability and longevity of the actuators. SMAs can experience fatigue over time due to repeated phase transformations, which can lead to a decrease in their performance. This fatigue can be exacerbated by factors such as high temperatures, mechanical stress, and environmental conditions. To mitigate these issues, researchers have investigated the use of advanced materials and coatings that can enhance the fatigue resistance of SMAs. For example, the use of protective coatings can reduce the impact of environmental factors and improve the overall lifespan of the SMA actuators. However, the development and implementation of such materials can be complex and may require additional research and testing.

In addition to the technical challenges, the integration of SMAs into exoskeletons also requires careful consideration of the control strategies used to manage the actuation. The nonlinear behavior of SMAs makes them difficult to control, as their response to temperature changes is not always predictable. Traditional control methods, such as PID controllers, may not be sufficient to achieve the desired performance in SMA-based exoskeletons. Researchers have explored the use of advanced control algorithms, including adaptive control and model predictive control, to improve the performance of SMA actuators. These control strategies can help to compensate for the nonlinear behavior of SMAs and ensure that the exoskeleton operates smoothly and efficiently.

Despite these challenges, the potential benefits of using SMAs in exoskeletons make them a promising technology for future developments. The ability of SMAs to generate high forces and displacements, combined with their lightweight and compact design, makes them an attractive option for a wide range of applications. For example, the use of SMA actuators in exoskeletons can enable the development of more intuitive and responsive devices that can better support users in their daily activities. Additionally, the integration of SMAs into exoskeletons can lead to the creation of more energy-efficient and cost-effective devices, as SMAs can reduce the need for complex mechanical systems.

In conclusion, the use of shape-memory alloys (SMAs) in exoskeletons offers significant potential for creating high-force and high-displacement actuators. However, the integration of SMAs into exoskeletons presents several challenges, including inconsistent force output, the need for reliable temperature control systems, and the requirement for efficient power sources. Addressing these challenges will require further research and development, as well as the exploration of advanced control strategies and materials. Despite these challenges, the unique properties of SMAs make them a promising technology for the future of exoskeleton design, and continued advancements in this field are likely to lead to more effective and reliable devices.

### 4.4 Electroadhesive (EA) Clutches

Electroadhesive (EA) clutches have emerged as a promising technology for integrating programmable stiffness modulation into soft actuators, particularly in the context of soft exoskeletons. These clutches utilize electrostatic forces to control the adhesion between surfaces, enabling dynamic changes in stiffness and deformation characteristics. This capability is critical for soft exoskeletons, which require adaptability to different tasks and user conditions, as well as the ability to provide assistance without compromising comfort or mobility. By leveraging EA clutches, designers can achieve a balance between structural rigidity and compliance, allowing for enhanced performance in tasks that demand variable mechanical responses.  

One of the primary roles of EA clutches in soft exoskeletons is to control deformation and enable dynamic shape changes. Soft exoskeletons, such as those designed for upper limb assistance, often rely on flexible materials that can conform to the user's anatomy. However, these materials may lack the ability to provide sufficient support or adjust to varying levels of force. EA clutches address this limitation by allowing for programmable adjustments in stiffness, ensuring that the exoskeleton can respond to the user's movements and environmental conditions. For example, in a soft exosuit designed to assist with shoulder movements, EA clutches can be used to modulate the tension in the fabric-based actuators, enabling the device to provide targeted support during specific motions while remaining compliant during others [22]. This programmable stiffness modulation is essential for applications where the exoskeleton must adapt to complex and variable tasks, such as lifting objects of different weights or navigating uneven terrain.  

The integration of EA clutches into soft actuators also facilitates the development of more intuitive and responsive exoskeleton systems. Traditional rigid actuators often require complex mechanical linkages and control systems to achieve variable stiffness, which can increase the weight and complexity of the device. In contrast, EA clutches offer a lightweight and compact solution for stiffness modulation, reducing the overall burden on the user. This is particularly beneficial in upper limb exoskeletons, where minimizing weight and bulk is crucial for long-term wearability. By incorporating EA clutches, designers can create exoskeletons that are not only more comfortable but also more adaptable to different user needs, such as varying levels of muscle strength or range of motion [63].  

Furthermore, the programmable nature of EA clutches allows for real-time adjustments based on sensor feedback, enhancing the exoskeleton's ability to respond to dynamic conditions. Soft exoskeletons often rely on multimodal sensing systems to monitor user intent and environmental factors, and EA clutches can be integrated into these systems to provide closed-loop control. For instance, in an exoskeleton designed for rehabilitation, sensors can detect changes in the user's muscle activity and adjust the stiffness of the EA clutches accordingly, ensuring that the device provides the appropriate level of assistance at each stage of the recovery process. This level of responsiveness is crucial for applications where the exoskeleton must adapt to the user's evolving capabilities, such as in stroke rehabilitation, where patients may experience fluctuations in motor function over time.  

Another key advantage of EA clutches is their ability to enable dynamic shape changes in soft exoskeletons, which is essential for tasks that require the device to conform to the user's movements. Unlike rigid actuators, which are often limited in their ability to adapt to complex geometries, soft exoskeletons with EA clutches can adjust their shape in response to the user's actions. This is particularly important in applications where the exoskeleton must interact with the user's body in a way that mimics natural movements, such as in hand rehabilitation. By using EA clutches to control the deformation of the exoskeleton's components, designers can create devices that are more intuitive and easier to use, reducing the cognitive load on the user [8].  

The use of EA clutches in soft actuators also opens up new possibilities for the design of modular and customizable exoskeleton systems. Traditional exoskeletons are often designed for specific tasks or user profiles, limiting their versatility. However, EA clutches can be incorporated into modular designs, allowing for the creation of exoskeletons that can be easily reconfigured to suit different applications. For example, an exoskeleton designed for industrial use could be adapted for rehabilitation by adjusting the stiffness of the EA clutches, enabling the device to provide support during repetitive tasks while also offering assistance during physical therapy sessions. This modularity not only enhances the adaptability of the exoskeleton but also reduces the need for multiple devices, making it a more cost-effective solution for users.  

In addition to their role in stiffness modulation and dynamic shape control, EA clutches can also contribute to the energy efficiency of soft exoskeletons. Traditional actuators often require continuous power to maintain a certain level of stiffness, which can lead to increased energy consumption and reduced battery life. In contrast, EA clutches can be designed to hold their position without requiring power, reducing the overall energy demands of the exoskeleton. This is particularly important in applications where the exoskeleton must be used for extended periods, such as in industrial or assistive settings. By minimizing energy consumption, EA clutches can help extend the operational time of the exoskeleton, making it more practical for real-world use.  

The integration of EA clutches into soft actuators also presents challenges that must be addressed in the design and implementation of exoskeleton systems. One of the primary challenges is ensuring the reliability and durability of the EA clutches under varying conditions. While EA clutches offer significant advantages in terms of programmable stiffness and energy efficiency, they must be able to withstand repeated use and exposure to different environments. This requires careful material selection and design optimization to ensure that the clutches can maintain their performance over time. Additionally, the control systems that manage the EA clutches must be robust and responsive to ensure that the exoskeleton can adapt to changing conditions in real-time.  

In conclusion, the integration of electroadhesive (EA) clutches into soft actuators represents a significant advancement in the design of upper limb exoskeletons. By enabling programmable stiffness modulation, dynamic shape changes, and real-time adaptability, EA clutches enhance the performance and usability of soft exoskeletons. These clutches offer a lightweight and compact solution for stiffness control, making them ideal for applications that require comfort and flexibility. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, the use of EA clutches is likely to play an increasingly important role in the development of more intelligent, responsive, and user-friendly devices.

### 4.5 Pneumatic Muscle Actuators (PMAs)

Pneumatic Muscle Actuators (PMAs) have emerged as a promising actuation technology for soft and continuum robots due to their lightweight, high power-to-weight ratio, and inherent compliance [20]. These actuators mimic the behavior of biological muscles by contracting when pressurized, making them ideal for applications that require natural human-robot interactions. PMAs are typically constructed using a braided fabric sleeve that encases a flexible inner chamber. When pressurized, the chamber expands radially, causing the actuator to contract in length, thereby generating a pulling force. This mechanism allows PMAs to deliver significant force while maintaining a soft and adaptable structure, which is essential for safe and effective human-robot collaboration [39].

Despite their advantages, PMAs face several challenges, particularly in dynamic control. One of the primary issues is hysteresis, which refers to the lag between the input pressure and the resulting force or displacement. This non-linear behavior can lead to inaccuracies in tracking and control, making it difficult to achieve precise movements. Additionally, the dynamic response of PMAs is often sluggish, which can hinder their performance in real-time applications. These challenges have prompted researchers to develop advanced control strategies to improve the tracking performance of PMAs [64].

To address the hysteresis issue, the Bouc-Wen hysteresis model has been widely employed. This model is a semi-empirical approach that captures the nonlinear dynamics of PMAs by incorporating a set of differential equations that describe the relationship between input pressure and output force. The Bouc-Wen model has been shown to be effective in predicting the hysteresis behavior of PMAs and improving their tracking performance [64]. By integrating this model into control systems, researchers can achieve more accurate and responsive control, which is crucial for applications requiring precise movements.

The Bouc-Wen model's effectiveness in improving the tracking performance of PMAs has been validated through various studies. For instance, in a study comparing different hysteresis models for PMAs, the Bouc-Wen model demonstrated superior accuracy in predicting the actuator's response to varying input pressures [64]. This improved accuracy allows for better control strategies, such as feedforward control and adaptive control, which can further enhance the performance of PMAs. The integration of the Bouc-Wen model into control systems has been shown to reduce tracking errors and improve the overall stability of the actuator, making it a valuable tool for researchers working with PMAs.

In addition to the Bouc-Wen model, other control techniques have been explored to address the challenges associated with PMAs. For example, the use of feedback control strategies, such as PID control and impedance control, has been investigated to improve the dynamic response of PMAs. These strategies involve continuously adjusting the input pressure based on real-time feedback from sensors, which can help mitigate the effects of hysteresis and improve the actuator's responsiveness. However, the effectiveness of these control techniques is often limited by the inherent non-linearities of PMAs, which can make it difficult to achieve consistent performance across different operating conditions [64].

Another challenge associated with PMAs is their limited range of motion and the difficulty in achieving precise control over their deformation. This is particularly problematic in applications that require fine-grained adjustments, such as robotic surgery or delicate object manipulation. To overcome these limitations, researchers have explored the use of hybrid actuation systems that combine PMAs with other actuation technologies, such as electric motors or shape-memory alloys. These hybrid systems can provide the benefits of PMAs, such as compliance and high power-to-weight ratio, while also offering the precision and control needed for complex tasks [64].

Moreover, the design and fabrication of PMAs require careful consideration of material selection and structural optimization. The choice of materials affects the actuator's durability, flexibility, and response time, while the structural design influences its force output and energy efficiency. For example, the use of high-strength, lightweight materials can enhance the actuator's performance, while a well-optimized structural design can improve its efficiency and reduce energy consumption [64].

In addition to their use in soft and continuum robots, PMAs have found applications in various other fields, including rehabilitation robotics, wearable assistive devices, and industrial automation. In rehabilitation robotics, PMAs are used to provide assistance to patients with motor impairments, helping them perform daily tasks and improve their mobility. The inherent compliance of PMAs makes them ideal for applications where safety is a primary concern, as they can adapt to the user's movements and reduce the risk of injury [64].

Despite the progress made in the development and application of PMAs, there are still several challenges that need to be addressed. One of the main challenges is the need for more robust and reliable control systems that can handle the non-linearities and hysteresis of PMAs. Additionally, the development of more efficient and durable materials is essential for improving the performance and longevity of PMAs. Finally, the integration of PMAs into complex robotic systems requires further research to ensure seamless interaction and optimal performance.

In conclusion, PMAs offer a promising solution for actuation in soft and continuum robots due to their lightweight, high power-to-weight ratio, and compliance. However, their performance is limited by challenges such as hysteresis and dynamic control. The Bouc-Wen hysteresis model has shown promise in improving the tracking performance of PMAs, and further research is needed to develop more robust control strategies and materials. As the field of robotics continues to evolve, the role of PMAs is likely to become increasingly important, driving the development of more advanced and capable robotic systems.

### 4.6 Pneumatic Artificial Muscles (PAMs)

Pneumatic Artificial Muscles (PAMs) have emerged as a promising actuation technology in the design of upper limb exoskeletons due to their inherent advantages such as lightweight, high power-to-weight ratio, and compliance, which make them well-suited for wearable robotic systems [47]. PAMs mimic the behavior of biological muscles by contracting when pressurized, allowing for natural and adaptive movements. Their use in exoskeletons is particularly beneficial for portable and energy-efficient systems, where traditional actuators may be too heavy or consume excessive power [21].

The integration of PAMs in exoskeletons offers several benefits that can significantly enhance the functionality and usability of these devices. One of the primary advantages of PAMs is their ability to provide bidirectional energy flow, which allows for both active assistance and passive support. This bidirectional capability is crucial for applications where the exoskeleton must adapt to varying user needs and environmental conditions. For example, in rehabilitation settings, PAMs can assist in both the active movement of the limb and the passive support during rest periods, promoting a more natural and comfortable user experience [7].

Moreover, PAMs are known for their energy efficiency, which is a critical factor in the design of portable exoskeletons. Traditional electric actuators often require significant energy to operate, especially when providing high forces or torques. In contrast, PAMs can generate substantial force with relatively low energy consumption, making them ideal for applications where battery life and power management are important considerations [65].

The advantages of PAMs in reducing muscle activity are another key consideration in their use for exoskeletons. By providing mechanical assistance, PAMs can reduce the workload on the user's muscles, thereby decreasing fatigue and improving overall performance. This is particularly beneficial in industrial settings where workers may be required to perform repetitive or strenuous tasks for extended periods. The use of PAMs in such applications can lead to reduced physical strain and a lower risk of musculoskeletal injuries [65].

In addition to their energy efficiency and muscle activity reduction, PAMs also contribute to the overall comfort and wearability of exoskeletons.
