Their soft and compliant nature allows for a more natural interaction between the exoskeleton and the user, reducing the risk of discomfort or injury. This is especially important in rehabilitation applications, where the exoskeleton must be worn for extended periods and must not interfere with the user's natural movements [47].

The integration of PAMs in exoskeletons also allows for the development of more compact and lightweight systems. Traditional actuators often require bulky mechanical components and complex control systems, which can increase the overall size and weight of the exoskeleton. In contrast, PAMs can be integrated into the exoskeleton's structure in a way that minimizes the impact on the user's mobility and comfort. This is particularly important for applications where the exoskeleton must be worn for long periods or used in dynamic environments [21].

Furthermore, PAMs can be designed to provide variable stiffness, which is a valuable feature for exoskeletons that need to adapt to different tasks and user conditions. By adjusting the pressure within the PAM, the stiffness can be modulated to provide the appropriate level of support for each task. This adaptability is crucial for ensuring that the exoskeleton can effectively assist the user in a wide range of activities, from delicate manipulations to more forceful movements [47].

The use of PAMs in exoskeletons also facilitates the development of more intuitive and responsive control systems. The compliance of PAMs allows for a more natural interaction between the user and the exoskeleton, reducing the need for complex control algorithms. This can lead to improved user experience and reduced cognitive load, as the user can focus more on the task at hand rather than on operating the exoskeleton [7].

In addition to these benefits, PAMs can be integrated with other actuation technologies to create hybrid systems that leverage the strengths of each component. For example, combining PAMs with traditional electric actuators can provide a balance between high force output and energy efficiency. This hybrid approach can be particularly useful in applications where the exoskeleton must perform both high-force and high-precision tasks [21].

The challenges associated with the use of PAMs in exoskeletons include issues related to control and actuation precision. Unlike traditional electric actuators, PAMs do not provide direct control over their output force and position, which can make it difficult to achieve precise movements. However, advances in control algorithms and sensor integration are helping to overcome these challenges, enabling more accurate and responsive control of PAM-based exoskeletons [47].

In conclusion, PAMs offer a range of advantages that make them well-suited for use in upper limb exoskeletons. Their lightweight, high power-to-weight ratio, and compliance make them ideal for portable and energy-efficient systems, while their ability to reduce muscle activity and provide bidirectional energy flow enhances their usability and effectiveness. As research in this area continues to advance, the integration of PAMs in exoskeletons is likely to become increasingly common, leading to the development of more advanced and user-friendly devices.

### 4.7 Soft Pneumatic Actuators

Soft pneumatic actuators have emerged as a promising technology in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design, offering a combination of flexibility, safety, and adaptability that is difficult to achieve with traditional rigid actuators [22]. These actuators are particularly useful in assistive devices for infants, where the need for gentle and controlled force and motion is paramount. The soft nature of these actuators allows them to conform to the delicate anatomy of infants while still providing the necessary assistance. This unique property makes them ideal for applications such as neonatal care, where the goal is to support the development of motor skills without causing harm [22].

One of the key advantages of soft pneumatic actuators is their ability to provide controlled force and motion. Unlike rigid actuators, which often require complex mechanisms to achieve precise control, soft actuators can be designed to have a more natural and intuitive interaction with the user. This is achieved through the use of compliant materials and flexible structures that can adapt to the user's movements. For example, in the design of assistive devices for infants, soft pneumatic actuators can be used to provide gentle resistance or assistance during specific movements, helping to strengthen muscles and improve coordination [22].

The design of soft pneumatic actuators involves careful consideration of material selection, actuator geometry, and control strategies. Materials such as silicone, rubber, and other flexible polymers are commonly used due to their ability to withstand repeated deformation without losing their structural integrity. The geometry of the actuator is also critical, as it determines the range of motion and the force output. For instance, the use of pleated or bellows-like structures can enhance the actuator's ability to generate force while maintaining flexibility [22].

Optimizing the performance of soft pneumatic actuators is a critical step in their development. This involves both simulation and physical testing to ensure that the actuators meet the required specifications. Simulation techniques, such as finite element analysis (FEA), can be used to model the behavior of the actuators under various conditions. These simulations help identify potential issues and guide the design process, reducing the need for extensive physical prototyping [22].

Physical testing is equally important, as it allows for the validation of simulation results and the identification of real-world performance characteristics. For example, in the development of assistive devices for infants, physical testing can help determine the optimal force output and the actuator's ability to provide consistent assistance over time. This is crucial for ensuring the safety and effectiveness of the device [22].

In addition to simulation and physical testing, the integration of sensors and control systems is essential for the effective operation of soft pneumatic actuators. Sensors can be used to monitor the actuator's performance and provide feedback for real-time adjustments. For instance, pressure sensors can be integrated into the actuator to monitor the internal pressure and ensure that the force output remains within safe limits [22].

Another important aspect of soft pneumatic actuator design is the use of advanced control strategies. These strategies are necessary to manage the complex interactions between the actuator and the user. For example, in the context of upper limb exoskeletons, the control system must be able to adjust the actuator's output based on the user's movements and the task requirements. This can be achieved through the use of adaptive control algorithms that can learn and respond to changes in the environment [22].

The use of soft pneumatic actuators in assistive devices for infants is a prime example of how these actuators can be tailored to meet specific needs. In these applications, the actuators must be able to provide gentle and controlled assistance while also being lightweight and easy to wear. This has led to the development of compact and modular actuator designs that can be integrated into various types of assistive devices [22].

Moreover, the integration of soft pneumatic actuators into exoskeletons has the potential to improve the overall user experience. By providing a more natural and intuitive interaction, these actuators can enhance the comfort and usability of the exoskeleton. This is particularly important in rehabilitation settings, where the goal is to encourage user engagement and promote recovery [22].

In conclusion, soft pneumatic actuators offer a unique combination of flexibility, safety, and adaptability that makes them well-suited for a wide range of applications in upper limb exoskeleton design. Through careful design, simulation, and physical testing, these actuators can be optimized to provide controlled force and motion, making them ideal for use in assistive devices for infants and other sensitive applications. As research in this area continues to advance, the potential for soft pneumatic actuators to improve the lives of users through enhanced mobility and support is becoming increasingly evident [22].

### 4.8 Bi-Stiffness Actuation (BSA)

Bi-Stiffness Actuation (BSA) is an innovative actuation concept that has emerged as a promising alternative to traditional series elastic actuators (SEAs) in the context of upper limb exoskeletons. BSA enables control over energy transfer timing and link decoupling, which are critical for achieving efficient and adaptive movement strategies. Unlike conventional SEAs, which rely on a single stiffness element to manage energy storage and release, BSA introduces the concept of variable stiffness, allowing actuators to dynamically adjust their mechanical properties based on the task requirements. This capability not only enhances the performance of upper limb exoskeletons but also addresses some of the limitations of traditional actuation systems, particularly in terms of energy efficiency and movement adaptability [19].

In traditional SEAs, the stiffness of the elastic element is fixed, which means that the actuator's response to external forces is predetermined. This can lead to suboptimal performance in scenarios where the exoskeleton must adapt to changing environmental conditions or user needs. BSA overcomes this limitation by incorporating two distinct stiffness states: a high-stiffness mode for energy-efficient force transmission and a low-stiffness mode for compliance and safety during human-robot interaction. This dual-stiffness approach enables the actuator to switch between modes based on the task demands, thereby optimizing energy usage and improving the overall performance of the exoskeleton [19].

One of the key advantages of BSA over traditional SEAs is its ability to improve movement strategy. In upper limb exoskeletons, the movement strategy refers to the way in which the exoskeleton interacts with the user's limb to assist or guide movement. By dynamically adjusting stiffness, BSA allows the exoskeleton to adapt its assistance based on the user's biomechanics and the specific task at hand. For example, during a task requiring high force output, the actuator can switch to a high-stiffness mode to provide maximum force transmission. Conversely, during a task requiring smooth and compliant interaction, the actuator can switch to a low-stiffness mode to ensure safety and comfort. This adaptability enhances the exoskeleton's ability to support a wide range of movements, making it more versatile and user-friendly [19].

In addition to improving movement strategy, BSA also offers significant advantages in terms of energy efficiency. Traditional SEAs often suffer from energy losses due to the fixed stiffness of the elastic element, which can lead to inefficiencies in energy storage and release. BSA addresses this issue by allowing the actuator to adjust its stiffness in real-time, thereby minimizing energy losses and optimizing energy usage. For instance, during low-force tasks, the actuator can operate in a low-stiffness mode, which reduces energy consumption. During high-force tasks, it can switch to a high-stiffness mode to maximize force transmission. This dynamic adjustment of stiffness ensures that the exoskeleton operates at optimal efficiency across a wide range of tasks, making it more suitable for long-term use [19].

The benefits of BSA extend beyond movement strategy and energy efficiency. By enabling link decoupling, BSA also enhances the exoskeleton's ability to handle complex tasks. Link decoupling refers to the ability of the actuator to isolate the movement of individual joints or links, which is essential for tasks requiring precise control. In traditional SEAs, the fixed stiffness of the elastic element can lead to coupling between different joints, which can complicate control and reduce precision. BSA overcomes this limitation by allowing the actuator to decouple the movement of individual joints, thereby improving control accuracy and reducing the risk of unintended movements. This capability is particularly important in upper limb exoskeletons, where precise control is essential for tasks such as grasping and manipulation [19].

Moreover, BSA contributes to the safety and comfort of the user by allowing the exoskeleton to adapt to the user's biomechanics and the environment. Traditional SEAs, with their fixed stiffness, can sometimes lead to discomfort or even injury if the exoskeleton is not properly calibrated to the user's needs. BSA addresses this issue by allowing the actuator to adjust its stiffness in response to the user's movements and the environment. For example, during tasks requiring a high degree of compliance, the actuator can operate in a low-stiffness mode to ensure a smooth and comfortable interaction. During tasks requiring high force, it can switch to a high-stiffness mode to provide the necessary support. This adaptability enhances the user's experience and reduces the risk of injury, making BSA a valuable addition to upper limb exoskeletons [19].

The integration of BSA into upper limb exoskeletons also opens up new possibilities for advanced control strategies. By providing the ability to adjust stiffness in real-time, BSA enables the development of more sophisticated control algorithms that can optimize performance based on the user's needs and the task requirements. For instance, machine learning algorithms can be used to predict the optimal stiffness settings for different tasks, thereby improving the exoskeleton's ability to adapt to changing conditions. This level of adaptability is essential for tasks that require a high degree of precision and control, such as rehabilitation and industrial applications [19].

In conclusion, Bi-Stiffness Actuation (BSA) represents a significant advancement in actuation technology for upper limb exoskeletons. By enabling control over energy transfer timing and link decoupling, BSA offers several advantages over traditional series elastic actuators, including improved movement strategy, energy efficiency, and user safety. The ability to dynamically adjust stiffness allows the exoskeleton to adapt to a wide range of tasks, making it more versatile and user-friendly. Furthermore, BSA enhances the safety and comfort of the user by allowing the exoskeleton to respond to the user's biomechanics and the environment. As research in this area continues to evolve, BSA is poised to play a crucial role in the development of next-generation upper limb exoskeletons that are more efficient, adaptive, and user-centric [19].

### 4.9 Variable Stiffness Actuators (VSAs)

Variable Stiffness Actuators (VSAs) have emerged as a critical component in the design of advanced upper limb exoskeletons, enabling dynamic and adaptive interactions between the robot and the user. Unlike traditional actuators that operate with fixed stiffness, VSAs can modulate their stiffness to match the changing demands of the environment and the user's movements. This capability is particularly important in human-robot interaction (HRI), where the exoskeleton must provide assistance without hindering natural movement or causing discomfort. The ability of VSAs to adjust their stiffness in real-time allows for safer and more intuitive control, making them a promising solution for enhancing the performance of upper limb exoskeletons.  

One of the key design features of VSAs is their ability to offer a wide range of stiffness. This is achieved through various mechanisms, including variable gearing, compliant elements, and hybrid actuation systems. For instance, some VSAs utilize a combination of rigid and flexible components to enable stiffness adjustment without significantly increasing the complexity or weight of the system [19]. The design of these actuators often involves a trade-off between stiffness range, energy efficiency, and mechanical complexity. However, recent advancements in materials and control algorithms have allowed for the development of VSAs that can achieve high stiffness variability with minimal energy consumption.  

Low-energy modulation is another critical aspect of VSA design. Traditional actuators often require significant energy to change their stiffness, which can be inefficient and impractical for long-term use in exoskeletons. In contrast, VSAs are designed to adjust their stiffness with minimal energy input. This is achieved through the use of passive elements, such as springs and dampers, which can store and release energy as needed. For example, the integration of elastic elements in VSAs allows for the modulation of stiffness by altering the tension or compression of these elements, which requires less power than active control mechanisms [66]. This low-energy modulation not only improves the energy efficiency of the exoskeleton but also enhances its portability and usability in real-world scenarios.  

The applications of VSAs in human-robot interaction are vast and varied. In the context of upper limb exoskeletons, VSAs can be used to provide adaptive assistance during tasks that require both precision and strength. For instance, during rehabilitation, VSAs can adjust their stiffness to match the user's level of effort, providing support when needed without restricting natural movement. This is particularly beneficial for patients recovering from stroke or other motor impairments, as it allows for a more personalized and effective rehabilitation process [6].  

In industrial settings, VSAs can enhance the performance of exoskeletons by providing dynamic support during repetitive or strenuous tasks. For example, in assembly line work, a VSA-driven exoskeleton can adjust its stiffness to match the user's movements, reducing fatigue and improving productivity [23]. This adaptability is crucial for ensuring that the exoskeleton can handle a wide range of tasks without compromising the user's comfort or safety.  

Another important application of VSAs is in assistive devices for individuals with motor disabilities. These actuators can provide the necessary support to help users perform daily activities while maintaining a natural and intuitive interaction with the device. For example, a VSA-based exoskeleton can adjust its stiffness to provide assistance during grasping or lifting tasks, making it easier for users to perform these actions without straining their muscles [53]. This adaptability is particularly important for users who have limited control over their movements, as it allows the exoskeleton to provide the right amount of assistance at the right time.  

The integration of VSAs into upper limb exoskeletons also presents several challenges. One of the main challenges is the need for advanced control algorithms that can accurately modulate the stiffness of the actuator in real-time. This requires a deep understanding of the user's movements and the environment in which the exoskeleton is operating. Researchers have explored various control strategies, including model-based control and machine learning, to improve the performance of VSAs in dynamic environments [17]. These strategies enable the exoskeleton to adapt to changing conditions, ensuring that the VSA can provide the appropriate level of assistance at all times.  

In addition to control challenges, the design of VSAs must also consider factors such as durability, weight, and user comfort. The materials used in the construction of VSAs must be able to withstand the mechanical stresses associated with repeated use while remaining lightweight and comfortable for the user. This has led to the development of novel materials and manufacturing techniques, such as the use of soft robotics and flexible components, to create VSAs that are both effective and user-friendly [8].  

The potential of VSAs in upper limb exoskeletons is further enhanced by their compatibility with emerging technologies such as artificial intelligence and sensor fusion. By combining VSAs with advanced sensors and control systems, researchers can create exoskeletons that not only adjust their stiffness but also learn from the user's behavior and adapt to their needs over time [2]. This integration of VSAs with AI and sensor technologies opens up new possibilities for improving the performance and usability of exoskeletons in both rehabilitation and industrial settings.  

In conclusion, variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) are a transformative technology in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design. Their ability to modulate stiffness offers significant advantages in terms of adaptability, energy efficiency, and user comfort. By enabling dynamic and intuitive human-robot interaction, VSAs have the potential to enhance the performance of exoskeletons in a wide range of applications, from rehabilitation to industrial assistance. As research in this area continues to advance, the integration of VSAs into upper limb exoskeletons is likely to play a crucial role in shaping the future of wearable robotic systems.

### 4.10 Dielectric Elastomer Actuators

Dielectric elastomer actuators (DEAs) represent a promising class of actuation technologies for upper limb exoskeletons due to their unique combination of high displacement, lightweight, and flexibility. Unlike traditional rigid actuators, DEAs are soft and compliant, allowing them to mimic the natural movement of human joints and provide a more seamless interaction between the exoskeleton and the user [15]. These actuators operate based on the principle of electrostatic attraction, where a dielectric material is sandwiched between two compliant electrodes. When a voltage is applied, the electrostatic force causes the dielectric material to deform, resulting in a mechanical actuation. This mechanism enables DEAs to generate significant displacement, making them ideal for applications that require large movements, such as assisting in arm and hand motions.

One of the key advantages of DEAs is their potential for high displacement, which is crucial for upper limb exoskeletons that need to assist in complex movements. The high displacement capability of DEAs is attributed to their ability to undergo large strain without significant stiffness, which allows for more natural and fluid movement. This is particularly beneficial in rehabilitation settings, where the exoskeleton must assist patients in regaining motor function. The ability to generate large displacements makes DEAs suitable for applications such as hand and finger movements, where precision and range of motion are essential [15].

However, the use of DEAs in exoskeletons is not without challenges. One of the main challenges is the issue of biasing, which refers to the need to maintain a pre-stretched state of the dielectric material to ensure optimal performance. Without proper biasing, the actuation efficiency of DEAs can be significantly reduced. This is because the pre-stretching of the dielectric material is essential to achieve the necessary electric field strength for effective actuation. The biasing process can be complex and requires careful consideration of the material properties and the mechanical constraints of the exoskeleton. Several studies have explored methods to improve biasing in DEAs, with a focus on developing novel materials and configurations that can enhance their performance [15].

A recent advancement in the field of DEAs is the integration of magnetorheological elastomers (MREs) as a novel biasing method. MREs are smart materials whose mechanical properties can be controlled by an external magnetic field. When incorporated into DEAs, MREs can provide a dynamic and tunable biasing mechanism, allowing for real-time adjustment of the actuation characteristics. This is particularly beneficial in exoskeletons, where the user's movements can vary significantly, and the actuation requirements can change dynamically. The use of MREs as a biasing method can enhance the adaptability and responsiveness of DEAs, making them more suitable for applications that require a high degree of customization and flexibility [15].

The integration of MREs into DEAs involves embedding the MREs within the dielectric material or using them as a separate layer to provide the necessary biasing. This approach allows for the adjustment of the mechanical properties of the DEA in response to changes in the magnetic field. For instance, when a magnetic field is applied, the MREs become stiffer, providing a higher biasing force that enhances the actuation performance of the DEA. Conversely, when the magnetic field is removed, the MREs become more compliant, reducing the biasing force and allowing for a more flexible actuation. This dynamic adjustment capability makes MREs an attractive solution for biasing DEAs in exoskeleton applications [15].

Several studies have demonstrated the potential of MREs in enhancing the performance of DEAs. For example, in a recent study, researchers developed a DEA with integrated MREs that showed improved actuation efficiency and response time compared to traditional DEAs [15]. The results of this study indicated that the use of MREs can significantly enhance the mechanical performance of DEAs, making them more suitable for use in upper limb exoskeletons. Additionally, the ability to dynamically adjust the biasing force allows for better adaptation to the user's movements, which is crucial for effective rehabilitation and assistance.

In addition to improving biasing, the integration of MREs can also enhance the overall performance of DEAs by providing additional mechanical support and stability. This is particularly important in exoskeleton applications, where the actuation system must be able to withstand the forces generated by the user's movements. The use of MREs can help distribute these forces more evenly, reducing the risk of mechanical failure and improving the durability of the exoskeleton. This is a critical consideration, as the long-term reliability of the actuation system is essential for the successful deployment of exoskeletons in clinical and industrial settings [15].

Despite the promising potential of DEAs and MREs in exoskeleton applications, there are still several challenges that need to be addressed. One of the primary challenges is the issue of material degradation over time. DEAs are typically made from soft and flexible materials, which can be susceptible to wear and tear due to repeated actuation. This can lead to a decrease in the performance of the actuation system over time, which can affect the reliability and longevity of the exoskeleton. To address this issue, researchers are exploring the use of more durable and resilient materials that can withstand the mechanical stresses associated with long-term use [15].

Another challenge is the need for precise control and feedback mechanisms to ensure that the DEAs operate effectively. Unlike traditional actuators, which can be easily controlled using well-established methods, DEAs require sophisticated control systems to manage the actuation process. This includes the need for accurate sensors to monitor the state of the DEA and the ability to adjust the actuation parameters in real-time. Several studies have investigated the use of advanced control algorithms and feedback mechanisms to improve the performance of DEAs in exoskeleton applications [15].

In conclusion, dielectric elastomer actuators offer a unique combination of high displacement and flexibility, making them a promising actuation technology for upper limb exoskeletons. The integration of magnetorheological elastomers as a novel biasing method can further enhance the performance and adaptability of DEAs, making them more suitable for applications that require dynamic and responsive actuation. While there are still several challenges to be addressed, the continued development of DEAs and MREs holds great potential for the future of exoskeleton technology. By overcoming these challenges, DEAs can play a significant role in advancing the field of upper limb exoskeletons and improving the lives of individuals who require assistance with mobility and rehabilitation.

### 4.11 Fabric-Based Pneumatic Actuators

Fabric-based pneumatic actuators have emerged as a promising technology in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design, offering a unique combination of flexibility, wearability, and functionality. These actuators are constructed using soft, textile-based materials, allowing for a more natural interaction between the exoskeleton and the human body. Unlike traditional rigid actuators, which often limit the range of motion and cause discomfort, fabric-based pneumatic actuators enable a more seamless integration with the user’s movements, making them particularly suitable for wearable robotic systems [59]. This section explores the design, performance, and advantages of fabric-based pneumatic actuators, with a particular focus on their applications in exosuits and the concept of Volume Transfer, which enhances both aesthetics and functionality.

One of the key advantages of fabric-based pneumatic actuators is their inherent flexibility. Traditional actuators, such as hydraulic or electric motors, are often bulky and rigid, which can restrict the user’s natural movements and reduce the exoskeleton’s overall effectiveness. In contrast, fabric-based pneumatic actuators use air as the primary medium for generating force, allowing them to deform and conform to the body’s contours. This flexibility is essential for upper limb exoskeletons, where the joints must move freely to enable a wide range of motions. For instance, in the development of a fabric-based pneumatic exosuit for knee extension assistance, researchers were able to design a system that is only 26mm in profile and can wrap around almost half of the leg while still providing adequate torque [59]. This design not only improves the user’s comfort but also enhances the exosuit’s overall performance by allowing for a more natural gait.

Another critical aspect of fabric-based pneumatic actuators is their wearability. Traditional exoskeletons often require significant adjustments to fit the user’s body, and they can be uncomfortable to wear for extended periods. Fabric-based pneumatic actuators, on the other hand, are lightweight and can be tailored to fit a wide range of body types. This adaptability is crucial for upper limb exoskeletons, which must accommodate different users with varying anatomical characteristics. The use of soft, breathable materials in these actuators also helps to reduce the risk of skin irritation and pressure sores, making them more suitable for long-term use. Furthermore, the design of fabric-based pneumatic actuators allows for a more aesthetically pleasing appearance, as they can be integrated into clothing or worn under regular attire without drawing attention [59]. This is particularly important in applications where the exoskeleton is used in daily life, such as assistive devices for individuals with motor impairments.

The concept of Volume Transfer represents a significant innovation in the design of fabric-based pneumatic actuators. Volume Transfer refers to the process of transferring the volume of pneumatic actuators beyond the garment’s profile to the inside, allowing for a more concealed and streamlined appearance. This approach addresses the common challenge of balancing functionality with aesthetics in wearable robotic systems. By moving the actuator’s volume internally, the exosuit can maintain a low profile while still delivering the necessary force and torque for movement assistance. For example, in the development of a fabric-based pneumatic exosuit for knee extension assistance, researchers utilized Volume Transfer to conceal the actuator within the garment, resulting in a design that is both functional and aesthetically pleasing [59]. This concept not only improves the user’s comfort and confidence but also enhances the exosuit’s overall performance by reducing the mechanical resistance that can interfere with natural movements.

In addition to their flexibility and wearability, fabric-based pneumatic actuators offer several functional advantages. One of the most notable benefits is their ability to generate significant force while maintaining a lightweight and compact design. For instance, a fabric-based pneumatic exosuit was able to generate a torque of 7.6 Nm at a pressure of 90 kPa, demonstrating the potential of these actuators to provide substantial assistance without adding excessive weight to the user. This is particularly important for upper limb exoskeletons, where minimizing the weight of the device is crucial for long-term use. Moreover, the use of pneumatic actuators allows for precise control of the force and torque delivered to the user, enabling the exoskeleton to adapt to different tasks and movement patterns. This level of adaptability is essential for applications such as rehabilitation, where the exoskeleton must provide varying levels of assistance depending on the user’s condition and needs.

The design of fabric-based pneumatic actuators also allows for greater customization and personalization. Unlike traditional actuators, which are often standardized and difficult to modify, fabric-based actuators can be tailored to the specific requirements of the user. This is achieved through the use of flexible, modular components that can be adjusted or replaced as needed. For example, in the development of a fabric-based pneumatic exosuit, researchers were able to optimize the actuator’s parameters using a mathematical model and simulation, avoiding the need for multiple prototype iterations [59]. This approach not only reduces the time and cost associated with the design process but also allows for a more efficient and effective integration of the actuator into the exosuit.

In conclusion, fabric-based pneumatic actuators represent a significant advancement in the field of upper limb exoskeleton design. Their flexibility, wearability, and functionality make them an ideal choice for a wide range of applications, from rehabilitation to industrial assistance. The concept of Volume Transfer further enhances their appeal by addressing the challenges of aesthetics and functionality in wearable robotic systems. As research in this area continues to evolve, it is likely that fabric-based pneumatic actuators will play an increasingly important role in the development of next-generation exoskeletons that are more comfortable, adaptable, and effective for users.

### 4.12 Hysteresis Compensation Techniques

Hysteresis is a significant challenge in the operation of pneumatic and soft actuators, as it introduces non-linearities and reduces the accuracy and responsiveness of the actuation system. Hysteresis refers to the lag between the input and output of a system, where the output depends not only on the current input but also on the history of inputs. In the context of exoskeleton actuators, this can lead to reduced tracking accuracy, increased response time, and compromised user interaction, making it a critical issue to address in the design and control of upper limb exoskeletons. Various hysteresis compensation techniques have been proposed to mitigate these effects, with a particular focus on adaptive control and model-based feedforward approaches. These methods aim to improve the performance of pneumatic and soft actuators by reducing the impact of hysteresis on system dynamics.

Adaptive control techniques are one of the most promising approaches for hysteresis compensation in soft and pneumatic actuators. These methods involve the use of online learning algorithms to adjust the control parameters in real-time based on the system's response. By continuously adapting to changes in the actuator's behavior, adaptive control can effectively reduce the effects of hysteresis and improve tracking accuracy. For instance, in the context of lower-limb exoskeletons, an adaptive feedback regulator has been developed to ensure stability and regulate desired walking velocity under model uncertainty [67]. This approach uses neural networks to compensate for unknown changes in model parameters and external disturbances, thereby improving the overall performance of the actuation system. In the case of soft exoskeletons, adaptive control strategies have also been implemented to enhance the accuracy of force and torque delivery, ensuring that the exoskeleton provides consistent assistance to the user [68].

Model-based feedforward techniques are another key strategy for hysteresis compensation in pneumatic and soft actuators. These methods rely on mathematical models of the actuator's behavior to predict and counteract the effects of hysteresis. By incorporating these models into the control system, feedforward techniques can improve the accuracy of the actuator's response and reduce tracking errors. One notable example of this approach is the use of the Bouc-Wen hysteresis model in the control of pneumatic actuators, which has been shown to significantly improve the tracking performance of soft exoskeletons [64]. The Bouc-Wen model accounts for the non-linear behavior of the actuator by capturing the relationship between the input and output over time, allowing the control system to anticipate and compensate for hysteresis effects. This model has been widely used in the design of control strategies for soft robotic systems, including exoskeletons, where it has been shown to enhance the accuracy of force and motion control [64].

In addition to adaptive control and model-based feedforward techniques, other hysteresis compensation strategies have been explored to improve the performance of pneumatic and soft actuators. For example, the use of hysteresis compensation algorithms has been integrated into the control systems of exoskeletons to improve their response speed and tracking accuracy [69]. These algorithms typically involve the incorporation of additional feedback loops or the use of advanced signal processing techniques to reduce the impact of hysteresis on the system's performance. One such approach is the use of adaptive control algorithms that adjust the actuator's response based on real-time sensor data, allowing for dynamic compensation of hysteresis effects. This has been demonstrated in the context of robotic exoskeletons, where the integration of adaptive control has led to improved tracking performance and reduced response time [70].

The effectiveness of hysteresis compensation techniques in improving tracking accuracy and response speed has been validated through numerous experimental studies. For instance, in the design of a soft pneumatic actuator, the integration of hysteresis compensation algorithms has been shown to significantly reduce the tracking error and improve the responsiveness of the actuator [39]. Similarly, in the case of a pneumatic muscle actuator, the use of model-based feedforward techniques has been demonstrated to enhance the accuracy of the actuator's response, ensuring that it delivers the desired force and motion with minimal delay [20]. These results highlight the importance of hysteresis compensation in ensuring the optimal performance of pneumatic and soft actuators in exoskeleton systems.

Moreover, the application of hysteresis compensation techniques extends beyond the design of individual actuators to the overall control of the exoskeleton. For example, in the development of a closed-loop control system for a lower-limb exoskeleton, hysteresis compensation has been integrated to improve the accuracy of the system's interaction forces and reduce the impact of hysteresis on the user's comfort [71]. This approach has been shown to significantly improve the transparency of the exoskeleton, allowing for a more natural and intuitive interaction between the user and the device. In addition, the use of hysteresis compensation techniques in the control of soft exoskeletons has been demonstrated to enhance the user's experience by reducing the mechanical resistance and improving the overall performance of the actuator [68].

In conclusion, hysteresis compensation techniques play a crucial role in improving the performance of pneumatic and soft actuators in upper limb exoskeletons. By addressing the challenges of hysteresis through adaptive control, model-based feedforward techniques, and other compensation strategies, these methods can significantly enhance the accuracy, responsiveness, and overall usability of exoskeleton systems. The integration of hysteresis compensation into the control and design of exoskeletons not only improves their performance but also enhances the user's experience, making them more effective in rehabilitation, industrial, and assistive applications. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, the development and refinement of hysteresis compensation techniques will remain an important area of research and innovation.

### 4.13 Optimization of Actuator Performance

Optimizing actuator performance is a critical aspect of upper limb exoskeleton design, as it directly influences the effectiveness, efficiency, and usability of the system. Actuators must balance multiple competing requirements, including force and torque output, bendability, controllability, and energy efficiency. Achieving this balance requires advanced optimization techniques that consider both the mechanical properties of the actuator and the dynamic needs of the user. Methods such as convex optimization, finite element analysis (FEA), and data-driven approaches have emerged as powerful tools for improving actuator performance in exoskeletons.

Convex optimization is a key method for optimizing actuator performance, particularly in scenarios where the goal is to minimize energy consumption while maximizing output. This technique involves formulating the optimization problem as a convex function, which ensures that any local minimum is also a global minimum, making it computationally efficient and reliable [16]. In the context of exoskeletons, convex optimization can be applied to minimize the energy required to achieve a desired torque profile while ensuring that the actuator remains within its operational limits. This is particularly important for actuators such as series elastic actuators (SEAs), where the compliance of the elastic element must be optimized to balance force output with energy efficiency [72]. By formulating the problem as a convex optimization task, designers can determine the optimal stiffness and damping parameters for the actuator, leading to improved performance and reduced energy consumption.

Finite element analysis (FEA) is another critical tool for optimizing actuator performance, especially for actuators with complex geometries or nonlinear behaviors. FEA allows designers to simulate the mechanical response of an actuator under various loading conditions, enabling them to identify potential failure points and optimize material distribution. For example, in the design of soft pneumatic actuators, FEA can be used to predict the deformation and stress distribution of the actuator under different pressure inputs [39]. By iteratively refining the design based on FEA results, designers can enhance the actuator's force output, durability, and controllability. This is particularly important for actuators used in rehabilitation, where the need for precise force control and long-term reliability is paramount.

Data-driven approaches have also gained traction in the optimization of actuator performance, particularly in the context of adaptive control systems. These approaches leverage real-time data from sensors and machine learning algorithms to dynamically adjust actuator parameters based on user feedback and environmental conditions. For instance, in the design of variable stiffness actuators (VSAs), data-driven methods can be used to estimate the optimal stiffness profile based on the user's movement patterns and task requirements [66]. This approach allows the actuator to adapt to different scenarios, improving both the efficiency and effectiveness of the exoskeleton. Furthermore, data-driven optimization can be integrated with online learning techniques to continuously refine the actuator's performance based on user interactions, leading to a more personalized and responsive system.

Balancing force, torque, bendability, and controllability is a fundamental challenge in actuator design, as each of these factors must be optimized to meet the specific requirements of the exoskeleton. For example, in the case of soft actuators used in hand exoskeletons, the ability to generate high forces while maintaining flexibility is crucial for effective assistance in daily tasks [53]. Here, optimization techniques must consider both the mechanical properties of the actuator material and the kinematic constraints of the human hand. By using a combination of FEA and data-driven methods, designers can fine-tune the actuator's geometry and material properties to achieve the desired balance between force output and flexibility.

Another important aspect of actuator optimization is the consideration of energy efficiency and robustness in uncertain environments. Exoskeletons often operate in dynamic and unpredictable settings, where the actuator must maintain performance despite variations in user input and environmental conditions. This requires optimization techniques that account for uncertainty and variability, such as robust optimization and stochastic programming. These methods ensure that the actuator can operate effectively under a wide range of conditions without sacrificing performance or safety. For instance, in the design of series elastic actuators, robust optimization can be used to determine the optimal stiffness and damping parameters that maintain performance despite variations in user movement and external disturbances [72].

The integration of optimization techniques with advanced control strategies is also essential for improving actuator performance. For example, in the design of impedance control systems, optimization can be used to determine the optimal impedance parameters that balance the need for force control with the requirement for user comfort and safety. This involves formulating the optimization problem in a way that considers both the mechanical properties of the actuator and the dynamic characteristics of the user. By using convex optimization or other advanced methods, designers can achieve a more efficient and effective control system that enhances the overall performance of the exoskeleton.

In addition to these technical considerations, the optimization of actuator performance must also take into account practical constraints such as weight, cost, and manufacturability. For example, in the development of lightweight exoskeletons, optimization techniques must be used to minimize the mass of the actuator while ensuring that it can generate the required force and torque. This involves a trade-off between the mechanical properties of the actuator and the constraints of the overall system. By using finite element analysis and data-driven methods, designers can explore a wide range of design options and select the most optimal solution that meets all the requirements.

Overall, the optimization of actuator performance is a complex and multidisciplinary task that requires the integration of advanced analytical and computational techniques. By leveraging methods such as convex optimization, finite element analysis, and data-driven approaches, designers can achieve a balanced and efficient actuator that meets the specific needs of the exoskeleton. This not only enhances the performance of the system but also improves its usability, reliability, and adaptability in real-world applications. As the field of exoskeleton technology continues to evolve, the importance of actuator optimization will only grow, driving the development of more advanced and effective solutions for human-robot interaction.

### 4.14 Energy Efficiency and Robustness

Energy efficiency and robustness are two critical factors in the design of actuators for upper limb exoskeletons, influencing both the performance and usability of these systems. Actuators must not only provide the necessary force and torque to assist human movements but also do so in a manner that minimizes energy consumption and ensures reliable operation under varying conditions. The integration of energy-efficient and robust actuator designs is essential for the long-term success of upper limb exoskeletons, particularly in applications such as rehabilitation, industrial assistance, and daily living support. 

One of the key challenges in actuator design is the trade-off between energy efficiency and robustness. Energy efficiency refers to the ability of an actuator to perform tasks with minimal energy consumption, which is crucial for extending the operational time of exoskeletons, especially in portable systems. On the other hand, robustness refers to the ability of an actuator to maintain performance in the face of uncertainties, such as changes in load, environmental conditions, and user variability. While energy-efficient designs often aim to reduce power consumption, they may compromise the actuator's ability to handle unexpected disturbances or variations in task requirements. This trade-off necessitates a careful balance between efficiency and robustness in actuator design.

Convex optimization has emerged as a powerful tool for addressing these challenges by enabling the design of actuators that minimize energy consumption while maintaining acceptable levels of robustness. Convex optimization techniques allow designers to formulate and solve optimization problems that involve multiple constraints, such as actuator torque limits, power consumption, and dynamic response. By leveraging convex optimization, researchers can identify optimal actuator configurations that strike a balance between energy efficiency and robustness. For instance, in the context of upper limb exoskeletons, convex optimization can be used to determine the optimal placement and sizing of actuators, ensuring that they operate within their physical and thermal limits while delivering the required assistance. This approach not only enhances the energy efficiency of the system but also improves its reliability and safety [16].

Another important aspect of energy efficiency in actuator design is the consideration of actuator constraints. Actuators must operate within specific limits to avoid overheating, mechanical failure, or reduced lifespan. These constraints include maximum torque output, maximum speed, and thermal dissipation capabilities. The design of energy-efficient actuators must account for these constraints to ensure that the system can operate reliably over extended periods. For example, in the case of pneumatic actuators, which are commonly used in soft robotic exoskeletons, the choice of materials and the design of the actuation mechanism must be optimized to minimize energy losses while maintaining the required force output [20]. Similarly, for electric actuators, the use of high-efficiency motors and gear systems can significantly reduce energy consumption without compromising performance.

Robustness in actuator design is particularly important in uncertain environments, where the exoskeleton may encounter unexpected loads or changes in user behavior. In such scenarios, actuators must be able to adapt to varying conditions while maintaining stable and predictable performance. This requires the incorporation of robust control strategies and the use of actuators that are inherently resilient to disturbances. For instance, variable stiffness actuators (VSAs) offer a promising solution by allowing the actuator's stiffness to be adjusted in real-time, which can help to mitigate the effects of unexpected loads and improve the overall robustness of the system [66]. By dynamically adjusting stiffness, these actuators can provide the necessary compliance for safe human-robot interaction while maintaining sufficient rigidity to perform tasks effectively.

The importance of energy efficiency and robustness in actuator design is further highlighted by the need for exoskeletons to operate in diverse and unpredictable environments. For example, in rehabilitation settings, exoskeletons must be able to adapt to the changing needs of patients, who may have varying levels of mobility and strength. In industrial applications, exoskeletons must be able to handle a wide range of tasks and environmental conditions, from heavy lifting to precision assembly. In both cases, the actuator must be designed to operate efficiently and reliably, even when faced with unexpected challenges. This requires a holistic approach to actuator design, where energy efficiency and robustness are considered alongside other factors such as weight, size, and user comfort [73].

Moreover, the integration of energy-efficient and robust actuators into upper limb exoskeletons is closely tied to the development of advanced control strategies. These strategies must be capable of managing the complex interactions between the actuator, the exoskeleton, and the human user. For example, model predictive control (MPC) and adaptive control techniques can be used to optimize the actuator's performance in real-time, taking into account both energy efficiency and robustness [17]. By continuously adjusting the actuator's parameters based on sensor feedback, these control strategies can ensure that the system operates efficiently while maintaining the required level of robustness. This is particularly important in applications where the exoskeleton must respond to dynamic changes in the environment or user behavior.

In conclusion, the design of actuators for upper limb exoskeletons must prioritize both energy efficiency and robustness to ensure that these systems are practical, reliable, and effective in real-world applications. Convex optimization techniques can be used to optimize actuator configurations, while the consideration of actuator constraints and the use of robust control strategies can help to ensure reliable performance in uncertain environments. By addressing these challenges, researchers can develop exoskeletons that provide meaningful assistance to users while minimizing energy consumption and maximizing system longevity [16].

### 4.15 Integration with Control Systems

[32]

The integration of different actuator types with control systems is a critical aspect of upper limb exoskeleton design, as it directly impacts the performance, adaptability, and user experience of the device. Actuators such as series elastic actuators (SEAs), pneumatic actuators, and shape-memory alloys (SMAs) must be seamlessly incorporated into broader exoskeleton control architectures to achieve optimal functionality. This subsection explores the integration of various actuator types with control systems, including the use of PID, impedance control, and adaptive control techniques, while discussing the challenges and benefits of incorporating actuators into these architectures.

One of the most common control strategies used in upper limb exoskeletons is PID (Proportional-Integral-Derivative) control, which is widely employed due to its simplicity and effectiveness in maintaining desired trajectories. PID control adjusts the output based on the error between the desired and actual states, making it suitable for applications where precise positioning is required. However, PID control can struggle with nonlinear dynamics and external disturbances, which are common in exoskeleton systems. For instance, in the context of SEAs, PID control can be used to regulate the torque output of the actuator, but the compliance introduced by the elastic element may complicate the control process. This challenge is addressed in the paper "Quasi-Direct Drive Actuation for a Lightweight Hip Exoskeleton with High Backdrivability and High Bandwidth," where the authors demonstrate how careful actuator design can simplify the control algorithms and improve system performance [16].

Impedance control is another widely used technique in exoskeleton systems, particularly in applications where the interaction between the exoskeleton and the human user must be managed carefully. Impedance control adjusts the stiffness, damping, and inertia of the exoskeleton to match the user's movements, thereby improving safety and comfort. This approach is particularly useful in rehabilitation settings, where the exoskeleton must adapt to the user's changing physical state. The paper "Nonlinear Subsystem-based Adaptive Impedance Control of Physical Human-Robot-Environment Interaction in Contact-rich Tasks" presents a subsystem-based adaptive impedance control strategy that enhances the stability of the exoskeleton during complex interactions with the environment [15]. This method allows the exoskeleton to maintain a stable human-robot-environment interaction, even in dynamic and unpredictable scenarios.

Adaptive control techniques offer a more sophisticated approach to managing the integration of actuators with control systems, as they allow the exoskeleton to adjust its control parameters in real-time based on changes in the user's conditions or environmental factors. This is particularly important in applications where the user's physical state varies significantly, such as in rehabilitation or assistive settings. The paper "A Data-Driven Reinforcement Learning Solution Framework for Optimal and Adaptive Personalization of a Hip Exoskeleton" describes a reinforcement learning-based controller that personalizes the assistance provided to the user, demonstrating the potential of adaptive control techniques in improving the performance of exoskeleton systems [74]. This approach not only enhances the user's experience but also ensures that the exoskeleton can adapt to a wide range of tasks and environments.

The integration of actuators into broader exoskeleton control architectures presents several challenges, including the need for accurate modeling of the actuator dynamics, the management of complex interactions between the actuator and the human user, and the optimization of energy efficiency. These challenges are addressed in the paper "Model Predictive Control for Human-Centred Lower Limb Robotic Assistance," where the authors propose a model predictive control (MPC) architecture that considers the dynamic interactions between the exoskeleton and the user to provide optimal assistance [17]. This approach ensures that the exoskeleton can respond to the user's needs in real-time, while also maintaining stability and safety.

Despite these challenges, the integration of actuators with control systems offers numerous benefits, including improved performance, increased adaptability, and enhanced user comfort. The use of advanced control strategies such as PID, impedance control, and adaptive control allows exoskeleton systems to handle the complexities of human-robot interactions more effectively. For example, the paper "Haptic Transparency and Interaction Force Control for a Lower-Limb Exoskeleton" describes a closed-loop compensation method that improves the transparency and interaction force control of the exoskeleton, leading to a more natural and intuitive user experience [71]. This approach demonstrates how the integration of actuators with sophisticated control systems can significantly enhance the functionality of exoskeletons.

In addition to the technical challenges, the integration of actuators with control systems also requires careful consideration of the user's comfort and safety. The paper "Comfort-Centered Design of a Lightweight and Backdrivable Knee Exoskeleton" highlights the importance of designing actuators that are not only effective but also comfortable for the user [51]. By incorporating low-impedance mechanical transmissions and rolling knee joints, the authors demonstrate how the integration of actuators can be optimized to reduce user fatigue and improve overall performance.

Overall, the integration of different actuator types with control systems is a complex but essential aspect of upper limb exoskeleton design. By leveraging advanced control strategies such as PID, impedance control, and adaptive control, researchers can develop exoskeletons that are more responsive, adaptable, and user-friendly. The challenges associated with this integration are significant, but the benefits—such as improved performance, enhanced user comfort, and greater adaptability—make it a worthwhile endeavor. As the field of upper limb exoskeletons continues to evolve, the development of more sophisticated control systems will play a crucial role in advancing the capabilities of these devices and improving the lives of users.

## 5 Sensing and Feedback Systems

### 5.1 Electromyography (EMG) in Sensing and Feedback Systems

Electromyography (EMG) plays a crucial role in capturing muscle activity for intent recognition and control in upper limb exoskeletons, particularly in prosthetic and assistive devices. EMG involves measuring the electrical activity generated by muscle contractions, which can be used to infer the user's motor intentions and translate them into control signals for exoskeletons. This technology enables a direct interface between the user's neuromuscular system and the exoskeleton, allowing for more intuitive and responsive control. The integration of EMG in exoskeletons has been extensively explored in various studies, with a focus on improving usability, accuracy, and robustness in different application scenarios.

One of the primary applications of EMG in upper limb exoskeletons is in prosthetic devices, where it is used to control the movement of robotic limbs. By detecting the electrical signals from the user's residual muscles, EMG can provide real-time feedback about the intended motion, enabling the prosthetic limb to perform complex tasks with a high degree of precision. For example, the "Novel Time Domain Based Upper-Limb Prosthesis Control using Incremental Learning Approach" paper discusses the use of EMG signals in an online training system for upper limb prostheses, demonstrating improved performance in recognizing and executing a range of movements [75]. This approach leverages incremental learning to adapt to changes in the user's muscle activity, enhancing the overall usability and accuracy of the prosthetic control system.

In addition to prosthetics, EMG is also widely used in assistive devices to support individuals with motor impairments. For instance, the "Intelligent upper-limb exoskeleton integrated with soft wearable bioelectronics and deep-learning for human intention-driven strength augmentation based on sensory feedback" paper highlights the use of EMG in conjunction with deep learning algorithms to predict human intention and provide strength augmentation [2]. This system uses soft wearable sensors to collect real-time muscle signals, which are then processed by a cloud-based deep learning model to determine the user's intended movement. The exoskeleton then provides assistance accordingly, significantly enhancing the user's physical capabilities.

Despite its advantages, the use of EMG in exoskeletons presents several challenges, particularly in terms of robustness and accuracy. One of the main issues is the variability of EMG signals, which can be affected by factors such as electrode placement, skin condition, and user movement. This variability can lead to inconsistencies in the control signals, making it difficult to achieve reliable and consistent performance. To address this challenge, researchers have explored various approaches to improve the robustness of EMG-based control systems. For example, the "EMG-based Control Strategies of a Supernumerary Robotic Hand for the Rehabilitation of Sub-Acute Stroke Patients" paper discusses the use of surface EMG (sEMG) to control a supernumerary robotic hand, emphasizing the importance of accurate signal acquisition and interpretation [11]. The study highlights the need for advanced signal processing techniques to filter out noise and enhance the quality of the EMG signals.

Another challenge in EMG-based control is the need for calibration and adaptation to individual users. Since each person's muscle activity and movement patterns are unique, the control algorithms must be able to adapt to these differences to ensure optimal performance. This is particularly important in rehabilitation settings, where the user's condition may change over time. The "MyoSuite -- A contact-rich simulation suite for musculoskeletal motor control" paper provides a comprehensive framework for simulating musculoskeletal systems, which can be used to train and evaluate EMG-based control strategies [76]. The study demonstrates the potential of simulation-based approaches to improve the accuracy and robustness of EMG-based control systems by allowing for extensive testing and optimization.

To further enhance the usability and accuracy of EMG-based control, researchers have also explored the integration of multiple sensor modalities. For example, the "Multimodal Sensor Fusion for Enhanced Feedback" subsection of the survey discusses the combination of EMG with other sensors, such as force and tactile sensors, to provide a more comprehensive understanding of the user's intentions and movements [55]. This approach can help to compensate for the limitations of EMG alone, improving the overall performance of the exoskeleton control system.

Moreover, the development of machine learning and artificial intelligence techniques has significantly contributed to the advancement of EMG-based control systems. These techniques enable the extraction of meaningful patterns from EMG signals, improving the accuracy of intent recognition. For instance, the "A Data-Driven Reinforcement Learning Solution Framework for Optimal and Adaptive Personalization of a Hip Exoskeleton" paper presents a data-driven approach that uses reinforcement learning to optimize the control of a hip exoskeleton [74]. The study highlights the potential of machine learning algorithms to adapt to individual users and provide personalized assistance, enhancing the overall effectiveness of the exoskeleton.

In conclusion, EMG plays a vital role in capturing muscle activity for intent recognition and control in upper limb exoskeletons. Its applications in prosthetic and assistive devices have demonstrated significant potential in improving the usability and accuracy of these systems. However, challenges such as signal variability and the need for individual adaptation remain. By integrating advanced signal processing techniques, multimodal sensor fusion, and machine learning algorithms, researchers are working to overcome these challenges and enhance the performance of EMG-based control systems. As the field continues to evolve, the role of EMG in upper limb exoskeletons is expected to become even more prominent, paving the way for more intuitive and responsive exoskeleton systems.

### 5.2 Force and Tactile Sensing for Grasp and Manipulation

Force and tactile sensing play a crucial role in robotic and prosthetic hands, enabling precise and safe manipulation of objects. These sensing technologies provide critical feedback on grip force, object contact, and surface properties, which are essential for achieving dexterity and preventing damage to both the object and the user. Force sensors, tactile sensors, and mechanomyography (MMG) are commonly employed in these systems to enhance the interaction between the robotic or prosthetic hand and the environment. Each of these sensing modalities contributes uniquely to the overall performance of the system, and their integration is a key area of research in upper limb exoskeleton design.

Force sensors are fundamental to understanding the interaction between the robotic hand and the object being manipulated. These sensors measure the force applied during grasping and can provide real-time feedback to adjust the grip strength dynamically. For instance, in robotic hands, force sensors are often embedded in the fingertips or palm to detect the pressure exerted on the object. This information is vital for ensuring that the grip is neither too weak, which could lead to the object slipping, nor too strong, which could cause damage to the object or discomfort to the user. In the context of upper limb exoskeletons, force sensing is particularly important for applications such as rehabilitation, where the exoskeleton needs to provide the right amount of assistance without overloading the user’s muscles or joints [8].

Tactile sensors, on the other hand, go beyond force sensing by providing information about the texture, hardness, and shape of the object being manipulated. These sensors can be embedded in the fingers or palm of the robotic hand and can detect a wide range of tactile inputs, such as pressure, shear forces, and temperature. For example, in prosthetic hands, tactile sensors enable users to perceive the surface properties of objects, which is critical for tasks that require fine motor control, such as handling delicate items or writing. The integration of tactile sensors allows the robotic hand to adapt its grip based on the object’s characteristics, leading to improved safety and precision [21].

Mechanomyography (MMG) is another sensing technique that is gaining attention in the field of robotic and prosthetic hands. MMG measures the mechanical vibrations produced by muscle contractions, which can provide insights into the user’s intent and the state of their muscles. This information can be used to enhance the control of the robotic or prosthetic hand by predicting the user’s movement intentions and adjusting the assistance accordingly. For instance, in upper limb exoskeletons, MMG can be used to detect the user’s muscle activity and provide support during movements, ensuring that the exoskeleton aligns with the user’s natural motion patterns [11].

The combination of force, tactile, and MMG sensing can significantly enhance the performance of robotic and prosthetic hands. By integrating multiple sensor modalities, these systems can achieve a more comprehensive understanding of the interaction between the hand and the environment. For example, a robotic hand equipped with force sensors can detect the initial contact with an object, while tactile sensors can provide detailed information about the object’s surface. MMG can then be used to monitor the user’s muscle activity and adjust the assistance level dynamically. This multi-sensor approach not only improves the precision of the hand’s movements but also enhances the safety of the interaction, as it allows the system to detect and respond to unexpected changes in the environment [21].

In addition to improving performance, the use of force and tactile sensing in robotic and prosthetic hands also addresses the challenge of user adaptation. These sensors enable the system to adjust its behavior based on the user’s specific needs and preferences, which is particularly important in rehabilitation settings where patients may have varying levels of motor function. For example, in a rehabilitation exoskeleton, force and tactile sensors can be used to monitor the user’s progress and adjust the level of assistance accordingly. This personalized approach can lead to more effective rehabilitation outcomes and a better user experience [8].

Another important consideration in the design of robotic and prosthetic hands is the need for robust and reliable sensing technologies. The performance of these systems depends heavily on the accuracy and reliability of the sensors used. Force sensors must be able to withstand the mechanical stresses of repeated use, while tactile sensors must be able to provide consistent readings even under varying conditions. The integration of advanced materials and sensor technologies can help address these challenges, ensuring that the robotic or prosthetic hand operates reliably in a wide range of environments [21].

The application of force and tactile sensing in robotic and prosthetic hands is also extending to new areas such as virtual reality and telepresence. In these contexts, the ability to provide realistic haptic feedback is critical for creating immersive experiences. By incorporating force and tactile sensors, robotic hands can simulate the sensation of touch, allowing users to interact with virtual objects as if they were real. This has significant implications for rehabilitation, where virtual environments can be used to create engaging and interactive therapy sessions [42].

In conclusion, force and tactile sensing are essential components of robotic and prosthetic hands, enabling precise and safe manipulation of objects. These technologies provide critical feedback on grip force, object contact, and surface properties, which are essential for achieving dexterity and preventing damage to both the object and the user. The integration of multiple sensor modalities, such as force sensors, tactile sensors, and MMG, can significantly enhance the performance of these systems, leading to improved safety, precision, and user experience. As research in this area continues to advance, the development of more sophisticated and reliable sensing technologies will play a key role in the future of robotic and prosthetic hand design.

### 5.3 Vision-Based Sensing for Environmental and Object Awareness

Vision-based sensing has emerged as a critical component in enhancing the environmental and object awareness of upper limb exoskeletons, allowing these systems to better understand and interact with the user's surroundings. By integrating cameras, depth sensors, and other vision-based technologies, exoskeletons can perceive their environment in real-time, enabling tasks such as object identification, gesture recognition, and environmental mapping. This integration significantly improves the exoskeleton’s ability to assist users in complex and dynamic settings, making it more versatile and adaptive to real-world applications.

One of the key advantages of vision-based systems is their capacity for environmental perception. In upper limb exoskeletons, this involves the ability to detect and interpret the spatial layout of the surroundings. For example, depth sensors like RGB-D cameras can provide three-dimensional information about the environment, allowing the exoskeleton to recognize obstacles and navigate around them. This is particularly important in industrial or assistive scenarios where the exoskeleton must interact with objects or navigate through cluttered spaces. Studies have shown that integrating depth sensing technologies into exoskeleton systems can enhance the user’s ability to perform tasks that require precise spatial awareness [76].

In addition to environmental perception, vision-based sensing plays a crucial role in object identification. By using cameras and advanced image processing algorithms, exoskeletons can recognize objects in the user’s environment, which is vital for tasks involving object manipulation or interaction. For instance, in rehabilitation settings, the exoskeleton can identify specific objects that the user is trying to grasp, allowing it to provide targeted assistance. This functionality is particularly beneficial for individuals with motor impairments, as it enables the exoskeleton to support the user in performing tasks that would otherwise be challenging. Research in this area has demonstrated the potential of vision-based object recognition systems to improve the effectiveness of exoskeletons in assistive and rehabilitation applications [2].

Gesture recognition is another significant application of vision-based sensing in upper limb exoskeletons. By capturing and analyzing hand and arm movements, these systems can interpret the user’s intentions and respond accordingly. This is particularly useful in scenarios where the user may not have direct access to traditional control interfaces, such as in assistive or industrial settings. For example, in industrial environments, the exoskeleton can detect specific gestures that indicate the user’s desire to lift or move an object, enabling seamless and intuitive interaction. Studies have shown that integrating gesture recognition with exoskeletons can significantly improve the user experience by reducing the need for complex control interfaces and enhancing the system’s responsiveness to user commands [77].

Moreover, vision-based systems contribute to the development of more intelligent and adaptive exoskeletons. By continuously analyzing the user’s environment and movements, these systems can adjust their behavior to better suit the user’s needs. For example, if the exoskeleton detects that the user is attempting to perform a complex task, it can adjust its assistance level to provide the appropriate support. This adaptability is crucial for ensuring that the exoskeleton remains effective across a wide range of tasks and environments. Research in this area has highlighted the importance of integrating vision-based sensing with machine learning algorithms to enable real-time adaptation and decision-making [18].

Another benefit of vision-based sensing is its potential to enhance the safety of exoskeleton systems. By monitoring the user’s surroundings, the exoskeleton can detect potential hazards and take corrective actions to prevent accidents. For example, if the exoskeleton detects a sudden movement or an obstacle in the user’s path, it can adjust its assistance to avoid collisions. This level of situational awareness is particularly important in industrial settings where the exoskeleton may be used in close proximity to other machinery or personnel. Studies have shown that integrating vision-based systems into exoskeletons can significantly improve safety by reducing the risk of accidents and enhancing the user’s situational awareness [12].

Furthermore, the combination of vision-based sensing with other modalities, such as force and tactile sensors, can lead to more comprehensive and accurate perception of the environment. For example, by fusing data from vision sensors with force sensors, the exoskeleton can better understand the interaction between the user and the environment. This multi-modal approach allows the system to provide more precise and context-aware assistance, which is essential for tasks that require fine motor control and spatial awareness. Research in this area has demonstrated the potential of multimodal sensing to improve the performance and adaptability of exoskeleton systems [55].

In addition to enhancing the exoskeleton’s understanding of the environment, vision-based systems can also contribute to the development of more intuitive and user-friendly interfaces. By enabling the exoskeleton to interpret the user’s intentions through visual cues, these systems can reduce the cognitive load on the user and make the exoskeleton more accessible. This is particularly important for individuals with limited mobility or those who may have difficulty using traditional control interfaces.
